she cried, and waved her lily hand."</head>  IT was a sultry afternoon
towards the middle of August ; a glittering blue sea stretched between
Dover and the white coast of France&#x2014;a sea to allure the most
invincible landsmarj,--and hundreds of eager tourists were hurrying on
board the Calais-Douvres, as she lay alongside the betty, with her
steam up ; whilst heavily laden porters staggered across a second
gangway, carrying the enormous sacks that contained the Indian mail.
<p>Meanwhile the pleasure-seeking local population lined the upper
pier, and critically surveyed the great travelling horde as they
embarked for their autumn holiday. Proud and complacent the individual
who ncould recognize and indicate some well-known public character, be
it peer, politician, author, or actress, or even the hero or heroine
of some recent society scandal. Well-dressed women, soldierly looking
men, pretty girls, children and their nurses, and idle sailors, were
all more or less resting their elbows on the cold stone parapet, their
whole attention concentrated on the stream of people whose departure
they were superintending with such flattering interest. There go a
young couple, a bride and bridegroom, basely betrayed by their new
portmanteaux ; here comes a burly paterfamilias, followed by his wife
and a flock of chattering daughters ; parsons abound, singly or in
pairs, and strong-minded females in blue spectacles and <pb n="2     A
FAMILY LIKENESS."/>

gauze veils serenely occupy *the best places&#x2014;places which have been the just reward of a timely arrival&#x2014;whilst poor helpless creatures, with about twenty small parcels, are being mercilessly hustled to and fro in the throng. Numerous Americans, with prodigious Saratoga trunks, and a vast multitude of Cook's tourists, are all on the wing for the Continent. Two or three days of old-fashioned summer weather have set the world moving, the Calais-Douvres is absolutely crammed, and the crowd of spectators on the upper pier have never enjoyed a more satisfactory inspection.
<p>Some few favoured individuals are down alongside the steamer, taking leave of friends, and obtaining a nearer view of coronetted handbags, and Redfern's latest travelling costumes. A group of four stand close by the gangway, and have attracted more than one glance and smile, and the whispered comment, " Old Indians." The tall gentleman with aquiline nose, sweeping grey moustache, and a puggaree round his hat, is Colonel Romilly, a retired officer, who, after thirty-eight years' service, has luckily survived&#x2014;as so many fail to do&#x2014;to draw his pension of eleven hundred and twenty-five pounds per annum. Next to him is his wife, a lady with delicate features and frizzy hair, holding a large white-covered umbrella, with a frill like a pillow-case, over a somewhat florid bonnet&#x2014;a gay, vivacious, fussy little matron, who looks surprisingly juvenile to be the mother of the broad-shouldered young man beside her. Gerald Romilly is six and twenty, an officer in a Ghoorka regiment, and at present enjoying a year's leave in England. The family have come over from Folkestone to speed a friend, who is on the point of returning to the East&#x2014;a sharp-faced elderly matron, with keen eyes and a brisk manner, and undoubtedly an experienced traveller. Observe her neat bundle of wraps, her dust-cloak, umbrella, and handbag 1 She has no encumbrances, and possibly congratulates herself that she is not as others are, overwhelmed with hampers, cages, baskets, and bundles.</p>
<p>"This is a frightful time of year for you to be going back, Teresa," said Mrs. Romilly, with a slight sniff. "I cannot reconcile myself to it. But you will surely come home next May ? "</p>
<pb n="3     TO AWAIT AN ANSWER."/>
<p>"It will not be my fault if I am not home within twelve months," said Mrs. Mauders, emphatically. " Of course I know that I shall be grilled alive in the Red Sea, but it is only for a few days, and Tom's extravagance is so unbridled when I am away that, if I did not go back for a while, lie would have us all in the poor-house ; I am certain the servants are malting their fortunes&#x2014;they want ine to keep them in check," and she laughed a little dry, complacent laugh. " And really, with a boy at Eton, and three girls costing five hundred a year, and the rupee little better than a shilling -1 have to think of every pice."</p>
<p>" A hundred and thirty a year for each of the girls is preposterous ! " exclaimed Airs. Romilly, " and I have often said so, Tessy ; people always add on for Indian children, and I cannot see why. They don't eat more, and I can answer for it&#x2014;judging by Gerald&#x2014;that they don't learn more than others. I wonder you don't ask Mrs. G ace to reduce her terms for three of a family."</p>
<p>" Perhaps Mrs. Manders is afraid that she might reduce the children to match ? " suggested young Romilly, with a twinkle in his grey eyes. " When I was a little chap, and supposed to be receiving every indulgence, and more than a mother's care, I never had butter on my bread if I had an egg for breakfast ; the combination was considered too rielt ! The same applied to meat and pudding ; and in Iaent I abstained, sorely against my will, from milk and su•ar in my tea&#x2014;in fact, the only thing that they supplied, without stint, was physic."</p>
<p>" Yes, very likely ; but that was twenty years ago," answered the lady ; " people dare not treat children in that fashion nowadays&#x2014;iny young folk will be well cared for&#x2014;and, Berry, I particularly beg that you will not spoil them when they go to your mother for their Christmas holidays &#x2014;don't teach Aloy to smoke, and don't put nonsense into Nita's head, and tell her that she is pretty l</p>
<p>" Is she pretty, Mrs. Manders	he asked, with an air of ingenuous simplicity.
Mrs. Manders's only reply was a scornful stare, and, turning abruptly to his mother, she said--</p>
<p>" Now, Susan, let me find you quite settled down by the
<pb n="4     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>

time I come back. You have been nearly a year at home, and here you are, still haunting the offices of house-agents, and still going from pillar to post, a sort of genteel vagrant."
<p>"Quite true," acquiesced her son. "The Wandering Jew has a strong rival in my mother."</p>
<p>"But it is so difficult to make up one's mind," pleaded Mrs. Romilly, piteously. " You see, we have all the world to choose from; no ties now. If I had one or two married daughters-- "</p>
<p>"Instead of one unmarried and unruly son," interrupted Mrs. Manders.</p>
<p>"And advertisements are so deceptive. A splendid Elizabethan manor house is generally tumbling to pieces, and about ten miles from the nearest station ; a mansion in its own grounds is a wretched villa, close to a high-road. I went to look at a place the other day, and it was within a stone's throw of a large cemetery, and our nearest neighbours would have been the inmates of a military prison on one land, and a lunatic asylum on the other. And John here, likes the sea, and I like the country, and Gerald prefers London ; it is so lard to please every one. I think I shall wait till you come home, and establish myself near you."</p>
<p>"At any rate, we will stick to Folkestone for the present," put in Colonel Romilly. " It is a cheery sort of place, and we have a capital house, and can always meet our Indian friends as they go through."</p>
<p>"Yes, so you can," assented -Mrs. Manders, " and I expect your house is as usual, a sort of family hotel, or glorified dAk bungalow. I'm certain you have not shaken off your old habits."</p>
<p>"If our house is a family hotel, I should be glad to know what yours is at the present moment," inquired Gerald "Romilly. 11 The Kalka races are on now, and if I know anything of my godfather, every room is full, and not merely that, but the compound is white with tents. All he wants is a big sign-board inscribed, I The Alanders Arms, good entertainment for man and beast,' the whole surmounted by the family crest, a one-legged stork."</p>
<p>"A one-legged goose would be far more appropriate, and so I often tell Tom," said Mrs. Manders, with angry deci-
<pb n ="5     TO AWAIT AN ANSWER."/>

sion. " I dare say he has forty people to dinner and til"hu every day of his life ! "
<p>" I expect your khansamah is making a good thing of it," continued young Romilly, provokingly. " Think of all the eggs, and butter, and meat he charges for that he has never purchased ; think of the charcoal and Europe stores he is wasting and selling&#x2014;think--"</p>
<p>" Be quiet, Gerald ! " interrupted Mrs. Manders, irritably. " I have no patience to think of the robbery that I know is taking place."</p>
<p>" How I wish I was going back with you, Tessy 1 " ex-claimed Mrs. Romilly, with a mist in her blue eyes. "I do indeed ; I'd gladly go on board this minute, just as I am. I declare I would give ten pounds on the spot for the whiff of a bazar, or the sniff of a huka ! "</p>
<p>Mrs. Manders cast up her eyes and hands (containing bag and umbrella). " For mercy's sake, Susan, don't let people hear you saying such awful things ; they will think you are crazy. You were always absurdly fond of India."</p>
<p>" And am still. And why not ? My best and happiest years were spent out there. I shall never take root in this country, with its leaden skies and yellow fogs. Fancy Simla now&#x2014;the rains nearly over ! Jakko Dill would be just paradise. I believe I will go back with Gerald. There is a charm about India that, despite all its drawbacks, keeps a hold on one's heart."</p>
<p>At this juncture, the group was joined by a smart-looking woman in a neat tailor-made gown. She had a pair of bright brown eyes, a straight nose, and an animated expression, and but for a somewhat heavy jaw, you would have said that she was handsome, and at a little distance you would have said that she was young ; but if you looped searchingly into the face behind the spotted veil, you discovered that you were gazing into the countenance of a woman of eight and thirty. She had an admirable figure, and was faultlessly dressed, save for a large pair of solitaire diamond earrings, and an equally dazzling pin.</p>
<p>" My dear Airs. Romilly," she gushed, extending both hands, " I am delighted to meet you. I suppose you have come to see off some friends ?</p>
<pb n="6     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>

<p>"Yes. We came over from Folkestone to catch the boat train."</p>
<p>"Ah ? "&#x2014;with a quick, 'critical look at Mrs. Manders&#x2014;" and tae have come down to say good-bye to dear Lady Castle blarneys&#x2014;Dolly's cousin, you know ; she is going to Carlsbad "&#x2014;glancing to where a majestic dowager, in a long semi-regal mantle, was leaning over the side, conversing with a handsome middle-aged man, dressed with studied care, in a creaseless summer suit, and listening to whatever the countess was expounding, with his hands behind his back, his head slightly bent forward, his whole manner indicating charmed and respectful attention.</p>
<p>"You will come back with us and have tea, after the boat goes ? " said the new-coiner persuasively.</p>
<p>" Thank you," replied Mrs. Romilly, who never had been known to refuse tea ; "we shall be very glad--"</p>
<p>" Did you ever behold such a crowd ? " continued the other lady vivaciously.</p>
<p>"No, not often," rejoined Colonel Romilly ; this holiday-making is a new thing since my days, and with most people the great event of the year, and not f or one class, but for all. I believe I see our greengrocer on board. I wonder where he is going, and what he is looking forward to most?"</p>
<p>"That question is easily answered, sir," said his son promptly. "Ike is going to see Brussels sprouts and Jerusalem artichokes, and looking forward to French beans."
<p>"Don't be so silly, Gerald," said his mother, giving him a. playful little push. " You are like a clown in a circus." Then, addressing her Dover acquaintance, she said--</p>
<p>" I hear Mrs. Lightbody is crossing to-day, after all."</p><p> "Yes, she is on her way to Aix, ; and what do you think ? She has actually left the baby behind ! "</p>
<p>" I can't say I wonder at that," remarked young Romilly, emphatically.</p>
<p>" No, of course you don't," said Mrs. Carwithen, turning to him with a laugh, and now beginning to discuss a recent ball with considerable animation, once more leaving the other ladies to themselves. Meanwhile Mrs. Manders, who
<pb n="7     TO AWAIT AN ANSWER."/>

had been, so to speak, shouldered out of the conversation, had been making excellent use of her sharp black eyes ; presently she fixed her attention on the aristocratic-looking gentleman, with a flower in his button-hole, talking to the old lady with a pug under her arm, and a footman standing behind her.
<p>" Susan," she said, " just tell me who that man is. I am positive I recognize his face, but I cannot think where I have seen him ! "</p>
<p>" Oh, that "&#x2014;following his glance&#x2014;" is Mr. Carwithen ; but I am sure you have never seen him before, my dear ; he is not an old Indian."</p>
<p>"I never said he was ! " retorted the other tartly; "but, all the same, he has been in India."</p>
<p>"Yes, for a short time, twenty years ago. He hated the country, and we are always quarrelling about it. He says the very name of Lucknow makes hire feel queer."
<p>" Carwithen ! Lucknow ! Twenty years ago ! " repeated Mrs. Manders, slowly, with her eyes still fixed on the subject of her inquiry. "Ah, yes ! I thought so. So that is the man ! "</p>
<p>"Hush-sh-sh, Tessy ! This is his wife behind us, talking
to Gerald. Then you know him ? "</p><p> I know of him," expressively. " Dear me ! he wears
well, though getting a little grey, a little stout."</p>
,p>Mrs. Carwithen had pricked up her ears. The name of Carwithen is long, and not easily slurred over. She had caught it ; and more, she had heard Mrs. Manders exclaim, " So that is the man!"</p>
<p>Gerald Romilly had been at a loss to understand why Mrs. Carwithen had flushed up unaccountably, and given him several random answers ; he was not aware that she was absorbed in another conversation.</p>
<p>" Do tell me what you know about him, Teresa ! " pleaded Mrs. Romilly, in a low, eager voice. "It will be quite safe with me."</p>
<p>"So he is married ? " remarked her friend, calmly ignoring the question.</p>
<p>Yes, yes ; years ago. She has a large fortune. We met them at Cannes, and know them pretty well. Now
<pb n="8     A FAMILY LIKENESS"/>

come, do tell me ; I believe you have some joke about him."</p>
<p>" Joke ! " indignantly. " No, indeed."</p>
<p>"Well, story. Really, Tessy, I think you might tell
me."</p>
<p>"No, not just now "&#x2014;with a shake of her head&#x2014;" it's an old tale, twenty years old. You shall hear it when I come back ; it will keep very well till then. Here, I see they have all the mails on board. I must be off. Good-bye," shaking hands with Colonel Romilly. " I'll not forget your messages to Tom, nor the Nepaul pepper. Good-bye, Susan "&#x2014;kissing her,&#x2014;" mind you write often, and don't spoil the children. Good-bye, Gerald."</p>
<p>" What ! "---looking contemptuously at her proffered hand --" Mrs. Manders, do you not mean to kiss me too, considering that you used to kiss me when I was--"</p>
<p>"A troublesome urchin in pinafores, as sharp as a knitting-needle."</p>
<p>" If that was the case, I must have had more than one good point," he retorted, with. a smile.</p>
<p>" Well, well, well, I've no time to bandy jokes with you now ; you argue every question&#x2014;you are a loss to the Bar," impressing a hasty kiss on his bronzed cheek.</p>
<p>"Teresa, Teresa cried Mrs. Romilly, clutching her as she spoke ; "never mind Gerald&#x2014;you know him of old ; but give me just one word, one little hint."</p>
<p>But Theresa was already on board ; in another moment the gangway was withdrawn, the paddles churned, passengers waved handkerchiefs. Mrs. Manders waved her umbrella as she leant over and screamed--</p>
<p>"When I come back. Good-bye. I'll write from Port Said."</p>
<p>"Happy woman ! " exclaimed Mrs. Romilly, "with her face set towards Colaba Lighthouse. But really, John, she is as great a tease as when we were schoolfellows. I wonder what she knows about Mr. Carwithen. I declare I'd have gone over to Calais if I had thought she would have told me. I always mistrusted that man&#x2014;he has such shifty eyes ; and in spite of his sleek ways, I am certain his heart is no bigger than a gooseberry."</p>
<pb n="9     MISS BUDD PROPHESIES."/>

<p>"You are the most shamelessly inquisitive woman I know," said her husband, with a laugh ; " and, my dear Susan, surely the size of Carwithen's heart is of no particular consequence to you ! "</p>
<p>Another lady, even Mrs. Carwithen, who had made no remark whatever, and was still conversing in a distrait manner with Gerald Romilly, would gladly have accompanied Mrs. Manders all the way to Bombay, on the chance of sharing her secret, and of gleaning a few particulars concerning the earlier years of her handsome, well-born, inscrutable husband.</p></p></p></p></p></p></div><div id="002" type="chapter" n="2"><head>
CHAPTER II.<lb/>

<lb/>"For time will teach thee the truth."</head>
" IF you happen never to have seen one before, there is a real old Qui IIye," explained young Romilly, as he waved his hat wildly to Mrs. Manders. " She and my mother are great friends ; they were at school together, have lived in the same station for years, and shared houses in the hills. My mother could not get on without her ; she was the keeper of her purse and her conscience ; she assisted her to make up her mind, and to manage&#x2014;me ! I know that it was Mrs. Manders who put me into the service, and she has kept me down with an iron hand ever since I was the height of the table. Strange to say, I am uncommonly f and of her."
<p>"Who is Mrs. Manders ? " inquired Mrs. Carwithen, abstractedly, for she was still deep in certain curious conjectures.</p>
<p>"She is the wife of the collector of Undermore. I don't know if that will convey much to you."</p>
<p>" Oh, a collector's wife ! " scornfully.</p>
<p>"Why do you raise your eyebrows, Mrs. Carwithen ? A collector's wife is rather a swell, I can assure you. Old 1llanders rules a district four times the size of Dent. He is a little king in his own ground. On state occasions he rides
<pb n="10     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
on an elephant, and sits in a howdah with looking-glass panels, and Mrs. Manders walks out of a room before most of the ladies in the station."
<p>" Nonsense ! You don't mean to say so. Then they are quite in society, those sort of people ! We met a rather nice couple at Florence last year, and made some excursions to(, ,other, till the lady informed me that her husband was a collector, and, as I thought he had something to do with tickets or taxes, I just dropped them quietly."</p>
<p>" Tickets&#x2014;no ! but he has something to say to taxes. It is well for you that Mrs. Manders does not hear you," said young Romilly, with a laugh, as he followed Mrs. Carwithen and his parents up the pier.</p>
<p>" And this Mrs. Manders, when is she coming home continued Mrs. Carwithen.</p>
<p>" Some time next year, I hope."</p>
<p>" And she corresponds regularly with your mother " Yes, like clockwork&#x2014;never misses a mail."</p>
<p>" What marvellous energy ! Oh, here is Nancy Budd ! " as they were accosted by a somewhat buxom damsel, wearing an elaborate French costume, and a small flower-bed on her head, in the shape of a hat&#x2014;a toilette that was all very well for Trouville sands, but a little startling in Dover. Miss Budd's dancing black eyes and rather shiny features were brimming over with good humour, as she cordially accepted Mrs. Carwithen's invitation to come back to tea, and also the escort of Mrs. Carwithen's companion&#x2014;the lady having hurried on, and attached herself to Mrs. Romilly with a view of putting some searching questions to that artless and amiable matron.</p>
<p>" So you have just come back from abroad, Miss Budd said Mr. Romilly, by way of commencing conversation.</p>
<p>" Yes, from Trouville. We had a ripping time thee: ! At least, I can answer for myself."</p>
<p>"I am sure you enjoy yourself wherever you go," lie rejoined, with a glance at her commonplace, good-natured face, with its broad cheeks, broad nose, and broad smile.
"And only think ! Jessie Collins&#x2014;my dearest friend, you know&#x2014;turned up there most unexpectedly, and when we met at the Casino, and rushed into each other's arms,
<pb n="11     MISS BUDD PROPHESIES."/>
oh ! you should have seen how the people stared&#x2014;especially the French officers ! "
<p>" I'm sure there was not a man among them who did not wish he was between you."</p>
<p>" Now, really, Mr. Romilly, how can you be so very silly ? "</p>
<p>"Alas l I'm afraid I was born so. I don't mind making a small bet that you had not as great a show of pretty girls over there as we see here."</p>
<p>"No ; only they all look so frightfully dowdy, after Trouville. They have no style, no chic "&#x2014;she pronounced it cheek&#x2014;" about them."</p>
<p>"I can't say that that is much loss ! Pray who is the young lady in the pink frock and sailor hat ? "</p>
<p>"You don't admire her, do you ? She is a Miss Bigwither&#x2014;a nobody. Her face is not bad, but her feet would really frighten you. She is an Leiress though. Your mother will be making inquiries about her ; " and Miss Budd giggled significantly.</p>
<p>" My mother "&#x2014;with a laugh&#x2014;" is as anxions to what she calls 'get me settled' as if I was the eldest of a large family of plain daughters. And who is this charming vision with all her hair down ? "</p>
<p>" She is Miss Danvers&#x2014;one of a large family of pretty and penniless daughters. She is not the eldest either ; but Miss Violet Angel&#x2014;to give her her proper name."</p>
<p>" A most proper name, indeed ! " he exclaimed. "I've often heard of a Blue Devil, but this is the first time I have seen a Violet Angel."</p>
<p>"Upon my word, Mr. Romilly, you are too ridiculous," protested his companion, with another giggle. " How crowded the parade is to-day&#x2014;almost like the Park in the season ! I do love the Park, only I never know who all the smart-looking people are&#x2014;nor who are in the grand carriages and on the drags. I never happen to be with any
one who can point me out the celebrities, and it is so aggravating."</p>
<p>I can quite enter into your feelings Miss Budd. Don't you think a catalogue would be a capital invention ? It would sell like wildfire, or might be leased with the chairs--
<pb n="12     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
a nice little book, containing the names of all the what you call celebrities who frequent the Row, each name numbered, and each individual to wear his number, clearly printed, either on his back or round his neck, or on the top of his hat. The carriages I should simply placard&#x2014;no equipage, or well-known individual, society beauty, actress, preacher, racing man, cabinet minister, and so on, to be allowed to enter Hyde Park without their label, and I should leave the supervision in the hands of the police."
<p>" Now I know that you are j oking, as usual, Mr. Romilly ; but, for my part, I think it is a splendid idea, and I should like to see it brought into force."</p>
<p>" Then you would have no objection to wearing a ticket yourself, and,to be ticketed as Miss Nancy Budd from Dover ? "</p>
<p>" Not the smallest," she answered promptly. "No one would take the trouble to read it, unless&#x2014;unless I wore something very remarkable."</p>
<p>Romilly secretly glanced at her fantastic headgear, and wondered what her ideas of a remarkable article could be.</p>
<p>" Here comes Miss Montgomery ! " she proceeded in an excited tone. " I wonder if it is true that she is engaged to young Gray, of the Marines. For my part, I don't believe a word of it."</p>
<p>" Yes, it is a true bill," responded her companion.</p>
<p>" How can you possibly know? Why are you so certain ? "</p>
<p>" For a very simple reason. Because the man has given it out," he answered with an air of tranquil conviction.</p>
<p>" Now, really, Mr. Romilly, you are too horribly cynical ; it does not suit you at all. It is not considered the roan's place to publish an engagement."</p>
<p>" No, it is generally given out for him, My engagement has been announced repeatedly."</p>
<p>"That just shows me what I have always suspected." And she nodded her head till the roses on her hat became violently agitated.</p>
<p>" Suspected what ? "</p>
<p>"That you are a shocking, shocking flirt. Now, don't attempt to deny it ! " And she shook her parasol at him,
<pb n="13     MISS BUDD PROPHESIES."/>
with what she imagined to be quite a French air. " But, tell me how you like Folkestone. It must be frightfully
slow, living with ~~two old people. What do you do with
yourself all day?"
<p>" As to old people ; if you mean my father and mother,
they are in some ways younger than I am. I am never dull. I bathe and boat, and stroll over to Shorneliffe, and of an
evening I join the mob upon the Lees, and listen to all varieties of open-air music, and touching songs about ` love
and twilight.' "</p>
<p>"I wonder why love and twilight are always coupled
together ? " said Miss Budd, with her head on one side, and a would-be sentimental expression on her countenance&#x2014;where it was as much out of place as on the face of an eight-day clock. " Can you tell me, Mr. Romilly ? "</p>
<p>" I am sorry to say I am quite the last person to ask.
It's not a riddle, is it ? "</p>
<p>" No,"&#x2014;rather sharply&#x2014;" I never give riddles."</p>
<p>"I am afraid I cannot even hazard a conjecture, no more
than why eggs and bacon generally appear in company. Can you give me a reason for that ? "</p>
<p>" Oh, Mr. Romilly, what a dreadfully prosaic young man you are ! " she exclaimed impatiently. " The idea of comparing the two ! Eggs and bacon, indeed ! " And she
tossed her head, and once more set every rose on her hat in motion.</p>
<p>"They are by no means a bad combination. I can vouch for them, but I know nothing of the other."</p>
<p>" Do you mean to tell me seriously that you have never been in love&#x2014;not once	she demanded, with a searching
glance from her prominent black eyes. "Come now, think ! "</p>
<p>" Yes, I will think. Don't hurry me. Yes&#x2014;I remember that I was desperately in love once."</p>
<p>" Ah ! and where is the young lady now?"</p>
<p>" I really do not know ; and, only you might be scandalized, I would add&#x2014;or care."</p><p> " And was she pretty ? "</p>
"Yes. She had lovely hair, and beautiful sharp teeth. I recall them particularly, as she bit me severely in the arm
<pb n="14     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
during one of our numerous quarrels. We have not met since we were in pinafores."
<p>" I might have guessed the sort of answer I would get cried Miss Budd, indignantly. " You have not one scrap of sentiment in your whole composition ! "</p>
<p>" No, thank goodness ! no more than a sack of potatoes, or yonder old bathing-machine. However, my father and mother make up for my deficiencies."</p>
<p>How do you mean ? "</p>
<p>"Well, they are fond of places, and associations, and anniversaries, and old letters. They are very susceptible and terider-•hearted. A child could impose on either of them. They are quite a fortune to beggars and impecunious acquaintances."</p>
<p>"Then, if I were you, I would never trust them out alone," said bliss Budd. "Dote they must waste money ! "</p>
<p>" I don't know about wasting money ; but they don't get much return, I am ready to admit. Only last month, a stranger in the train burst into tears and told my father some harrowing tale, borrowed ten pounds to tide over a financial crisis, to be refunded in a week, got hastily out at the next station, and, of course, has -.ever been seen since ! As to my mother, bath-chair men, crossing-sweepers, and organ-grinders have but to assume an attitude of melancholy dejection, and out comes her purse ; and she actually gives cabbies double fare, on the understanding that they will buy a feed for the poor horse."</p>
<p>"Talking of charity and purses, they are getting up amateur theatricals here next week in aid of&#x2014;I forget what fund&#x2014;but you must promise to come over. They are sure to be capital. Tickets only ten shillings."</p>
<p>"Only ten shillings! Miss Budd, you seem to forget that you are talking to a penniless subaltern. I think that ten shillings is a round sum to pay to see the average amateur enjoy the delusion that he or she is acting."</p>
<p>"Now really, Mr. Romilly, you must not be so dreadfully sarcastic. Mr. Ff olliott is taking a part. I adore him on the stage. Of course, only on the stage," she repeated, with a giggle. "I am sure he is quite as good as any professional. Have yon ever seen him play ? "</p>
<pb n="15     MISS BUDD PROPHESIES."/>
<p>" Yes ; he is my cousin. I have seen him. play the devil and the fool pretty fairly. I suppose we can all do that if we try. I doubt if I should adore him, even on the stage. I have not met him since he was a lad. I believe he belongs to the Bachelor Club, and is considered a smart young man, and never wears less than a two-shilling flower in his button-hole ; and I know he has no desire to be introduced to his Indian relations."</p>
<p>Captain Ffolliott had spoken of his father as an old Hindoo colonel, and inquired "what was the difference between a Ghoorka and a gorilla ? " These remarks had come to Gerald's cars&#x2014;repeated by the usual good-natured friend&#x2014;and he felt a burning desire to administer a kicking to his talented cousin.</p>
<p>"No desire to be introduced to his Indian relations ! " echoed Miss Budd, in a tone of tragic protest. " Then all I can say, Mr. Romilly, is, that the loss is his."</p>
<p>" This is extremely kind of you, Miss Budd ; but I doubt if he would see it in your light. I know I am a fool to mind what he says or does."</p>
<p>" I think you are foolish in another fashion, if I may
say so."</p>
<p>" Miss Budd may say anything," was the gallant response. </p><p>"Then permit me to tell you that you are foolish to laugh at love and sentiment."</p>
<p>" I laugh at love!" in a tone of shocked surprise.</p>
<p>" Well, at any rate, you think you are invulnerable ; and, one fine day, Love will pay you out, and you will do some-thing that will astonish yourself and all your friends. Do you believe in prophets ? "</p>
<p>" Yes, in small profits and quick returns."</p>
<p>"There you go as usual, gibing and joking ! Well, you will see that, before you are thirty, you will do something desperately romantic. Yes ; you have it written in your face ; and then you will recall this walk on Dover Esplanade, and Nancy Budd."</p>
<p>" It will not require the fulfilment of her prophecy to recall bliss Budd to my recollection, and if this desperate romance ends in marriage, I most solemnly promise and vow to send sou a large cousignlnent of wedding-cake, carriage paid."
<pb n="16     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
<p>" Be sure you do ! though, somehow, I cannot picture you a married man; your spirits are far too high."</p>
<p>"Yes, at present ; but, no doubt, when the fate you predict has overtaken me, my spirits will be in the other extreme. I am to do something extraordinarily rash and romantic, am I not? Ah I see Mrs. Carwithen waving her parasol; shall we go in?"</p>
<p>"I wonder why she asked me to tea ? " said Miss Budd, speculatively, as they strolled towards their destination.</p>
<p>"That is easily answered; for the pleasure of your company."</p>
<p>"I doubt it, for it is a well-known fact that she rather snubs girls, and only likes married women, and what are called nice smart people."</p>
<p>"Surely, Miss Budd, you are both nice and smart ?"</p>
<p>"Am I ? "&#x2014;looking at him with a flattered smile. But not in the sense Mrs. Carwithen means. I have no grand relations. I am not intimate with Lady This or Lord That. I don't know even one duchess."</p>
<p>"And I am in the same unhappy position; so we are loth in the one boat."</p>
<p>"Oh, but you are very good family," she rejoined, with blunt candour. "I remember Mrs. Carwithen looking you up in Burke. She said that the Romillys had some splendid connections, and were really gentry, and had blue blood in their veins."</p>

<p>"Then, in that case, they will suit my people down to the ground," remarked her companion, m he followed her into one of the best houses on the parade, and upstairs into a cool drawing-room, whose open windows, with gay striped awnings, overlooked the sea-front, and through which floated a soft sea-breeze and the dreamy strains of the last new waltz.</p></p></p></p></p></p></div>

<pb n="17/"> <div id="003" type="chapter" n="3"><head>
CHAPTER III.<lb/>
PORTRAIT OF A LADY.<lb/>
<lb/>" She in thee,
<lb/>Calls back the lovely April of her prime."</head>

MRS. CARWITIIEN's drawing-room was not only cool, it appeared almost dim, after the glare of the parade. The lady of the house disliked a bold searching light, and was partial to veils, shaded lamps, and window-blinds. When the eye had become accustomed to the gloom, it discovered that Mrs. Carwithen was a woman of taste ; indeed, she considered the decoration of her house almost as important as the adornment of her person. The curtains and furniture were in old-fashioned brocade. There were valuable inlaid cabinets containing china and Dutch silver ; water-colours by well-known artists were displayed on easels ; fine Persian ru,s lay about the floor, where they were distributed with strtdied art ; and palms, flowers, and photographs were liberally scattered over the apartment. The most remarkable object, one that instantly caught the attention, was not a Louis-Quinze cabinet, a Japanese bronze, or even a Watteau screen ; it was a large, half-length painting, which was hung in the best light the rooms afforded&#x2014;the portrait of a beautiful young woman in a picturesque black hat. She had a quantity of reddish-brown hair, a pair of mischievous dark eyes, a delicate tip-tilted nose, and a perfect mouth and chin ; the whole pose indicated grace and spirit, with a spice of coquetry. The lady was very slim, and dressed in the fashion of a bygone day ; she wore a white gown, with a loosely knotted fichu, one taper hand was bare, and the other, gloved, rested on a silver-topped cane.
<p>It was altogether a striking picture ; the vivacity of the attitude, and the brilliant bewitching face captured the admiration of every, beholder. Mr. Romilly did not notice the partrait immediately, for he happened to be standing with his back to it, talking to Major Blundell about many mutual friends--for he and his wife had but recently
<pb n="18     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
returned from the North-west Provinces, and were already in high favour with Colonel and Mrs. Romilly. Presently a smart man-servant appeared, bearing tea on an antique silver tray ; the cups and saucers were old crown Derby ; the teapot dated from the reign of good Queen Anne, and everything about the equipage was in perfect keeping&#x2014;was ancient, elegant, and costly, and spoke of wealthy and refined ancestors. To tell the honest truth&#x2014;which same truth Mrs. Carwithen would sooner perish than reveal&#x2014;these family treasures had all been picked up at pawn-offices, curiosity shops, or auctions, by that ambitious lady, who subsequently imposed them on her guests as dear old heirlooms&#x2014;articles that had been among " Dolly's people " for generations. Whilst Mrs. Carwithen poured out tea, with fingers that glittered with diamond rings&#x2014;heirlooms also&#x2014;Mr. Romilly made himself useful in handing about tea and cake, and it was during an expedition to Miss Budd, cup in hand, that his eyes first fell upon the _picture, and he halted suddenly and exclaimed, " Hullo ! "
<p>"Well, Mr. Romilly, you seem knocked all of a heap, as they call it," said Miss Budd, with a titter. " Please bring me my tea, and stare at her afterwards ; she is not thirsty."</p>
<p>" Who is she ? Do you know her ? " he asked eagerly, feeling strangely attracted, and as if the eyes of the picture held him by a look.</p>
<p>"I believe she is Mr. Carwithen's ancestress ; his great-grandmother, Lady Juliet."</p>
<p>" His great-grandmother ! " echoed the young man, in a tone of profound disgust, his gaze still riveted on the portrait. " I was in hopes she was of the present time ; some girl got up in fancy-dress."</p>
<p>" Oh no," with a glance of complacency at this dead rival in her companion's attentions ; "you can see that it is an old painting ! She was a girl about a hundred and twenty years ago. What do you think of her ? "</p>
<p>"I think she has the most beautiful&#x2014;well, if not exactly beautiful&#x2014;bewitching, fascinating face, I ever saw "--still looking persistently at the portrait.</p>
<p>"You are not singular in your taste, though women seldom admire her, They say she has a saucy air, and a

cocticd nose ; but men are crazy about her ; they all fall in I be she was

<p>"Mr. Romilly shows his discrimination," observed Mrs. Carwithen, as she joined them. "I am always so enthusiastic about Lady Juliet myself. You never see that piquant aristocratic type now ; it went out with hoops and sedan-chairs. Have you noticed her delicate hands, her exquisite taper fingers ? "</p>
<p>"I have, and her whole air, a mixture of dignity and mischief. Mrs. Carwithen, I am her slave. If my mother could only find me a wife like her, I would marry to-morrow."</p>
<p>" I dare say you would, Mr. Romilly," rejoined Mrs. Carwithen, with a touch of severity in her voice, as she folded her arms and gazed at the picture. " She was a duke's daughter, and a celebrated beauty. The Carwithens are one of the oldest families in England, and date from the IIeptarchy, so that, although she married a Commoner, it was not considered a bad match&#x2014;quite the contrary. Of course there are Commoners and Commoners."</p>
<p>"Yes, and I am only a common Commoner. I keenly appreciate the difference," he replied, with exaggerated humility, which his hostess accepted in entire good faith.</p>
<p>" It is said that my husband resembles her to a remark-able degree," continued Mrs. Carwithen, with a complacent glance at her lord and master. " I see it myself. Don't you ?"</p>
<p>Gerald Romilly's glance followed hers. Yes, there was an undoubted resemblance in the set of the eyes, the shape of the forehead and the well-kept taper hand, with which Adolphus Carwithen was stroking his moustache, as he lay
back in his chair, and watched his wife with an air of lazy satisfaction.</p>
<pb n="20     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
<p>He was proud of his family, but she was ten times prouder. She was fond of trotting out his ancestors, and he could see that she was putting Lady Juliet through her paces now. Unlike most well-born men, he talked a great deal, in season and out of season, about his connections, his wealthy, well-born connections&#x2014;bien entendu, to one who was a chemist and another who was a lunatic he never alluded, and yet they were nearer kin than the Marquis of Castle Blarney, that he so constantly referred to as 11 my cousin." He was assiduously devoted to relations owning fine country seats, moors, and town houses ; he kept up an indefatigable correspondence with them, remembered their children's birthdays, was liberal with tickets and bouquets to their womenkind when in town, and they, seeing that he was an eminently presentable connection, who had married a rich wife and sown his wild oats, frequently invited the Dolly Carwithens, for the fashionable three days' visit. Mrs. Dolly wore obtrusive diamonds, had enormous ears, and was too effusively " a Carwithen ; " but, on the whole, she was a good-natured creature, and quite tolerable. Meanwhile, Mrs. Romilly and Mrs. Blundell were deep in conversation, the former listening with avidity to all the latest news from the North-west&#x2014;how the Greys had let their house in IIussouri, and gone to Cashmere ; how Miss Lamb had been shamefully jilted by Mr. Wolfe, and how clever Mrs. Fox had married off her two plain daughters.</p>
<p>"Now you see what these Anglo-Indians are like, Mrs. Carwithen! When once they get together, no other country has the smallest interest for them, exiles though they were," remarked Gerald Romilly. " My mother is perfectly happy in hearing about her old friends, and Major Blundell and my father are deep in the entries for the civil service cup."</p>

</p></p></p></p></p></div> <div id="004" type="chapter" n="4"><head>CHAPTER IV.<lb/>
A YOUNG MAN'S FANCY.<lb/>
"Why, this is very midsummer madness !"</head>
PEOPLE do not invariably retire from the Indian army, settle down at home, and live happy ever after, as is their Eastern day-drearn. At any rate, Colonel Romilly was an exception to the rule. Colonel Romilly, who was accustomed to an active life and congenial employment, found himself a
<pb n="25     A YOUNG MAN's FANCY."/>
hale and vigorous man of fifty-six, shelved for the remainder of his days. Yes, it was a sharp charge to sink suddenly from tieing a person of considerable social importance, with a full life of many friends, into the role of an old fogey, with few acquaintances, and nothing to do. IJ was too late to turn his attention to farming or politics, or any special fad ; all his tastes were connected with another hemisphere and another mode of existence. To meet the boat, to read the papers, to go trivial household messages&#x2014;how his former chuprassis would have stared and sneered&#x2014;went but a short way towards filling in the great empty hours, until it was time for him to drop in at the club for a rubber of whist. Dirs. Romilly, too, missed her large establishment, her ponies, her garden, and her friends. But since Gerald had been at home, life had been busier, brisker, and better ; and Colonel Romilly secretly echoed his wife's constant wish, that " Gerald would remain in England and settle down." He was their only child, and a distant relation had recently left him three hundred a year. Why should he go back to India ? If they could have accompanied him it would have been a different thing ; but, after having taken a solemn farewell of the country, after being entertained and feted and loaded with parting gifts, after scattering their house-hold gods and ancient retainers to the four winds, how could they return ?
<p>As Gerald and his mother strolled up and down the bees one semi-tropical afternoon, she was urginhim with all
the eloquence at her command to leave the-service
, and to
make his home with them.</p>
<p>" In another month your leave will be up, and you will be going thousand; of miles away, and goodness knows when
we shall see you again.	We might as well have no son,'' </p>
<p>" But, my dear mother, you surely would not wish me to leave the service at twenty-six, and turn into a loafer at home?"</p>
<p>"You need not loaf!"
protested his mother, with asperity,
" Then can you suggest what I am to do ? I am fond of my profession and of my regiment, and I am not clever enough to carve out a fresh career."</p>
<pb n="26     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
<p>"You might marry," said Mrs. Itomiily, briskly. <p>"So I might ; but on what?"</p>
<p>"Why, of course you have something ; and she would have something."</p>
<p>"And supposing that her face was her fortune?"</p>
<p>" I am not going to suppose anything so ridiculous. I know dozens of nice girls--"</p>
<p>" For that matter, so do I. And you, my dear mother, see in every nice girl a possible daughter-in-law."</p>
<p>" And pray why not ? "</p>
<p>"I wonder you are so anxious to get rid of me. You seem to forget that--"</p>

My son is my son till he gets him a wife ;
My daughter's my daugliter all the days of her life."'

<p>" Now, Gerald, you are too provoking. I want you to marry some pleasant, ladylike girl, with a little money ; I am certain to like her. No one ever said that I was hard to get on with ; at Julna I was the only lady who never quarrelled with Mrs. Bickers ; I simply would not fight, not even when she managed the mutton club, and gave me only the legs. And if you had a nice country place, where you could shoot and farm, I would come and live near you ; it would give me a new interest in life."</p>
<p>" But if you were to take up some pet charity&#x2014;or even politics, or the Primrose League&#x2014;would not that answer just as well?"</p>
<p>" Really, Gerald, I had no idea you were so heartless ! Primrose League ! as if it would be the same as my only child. Charity ! my charity begins at home."</p>
<p>" Oh ! and that old impostor, yesterday, that borrowed two pounds to bury his dead wife, and, in a fit of absence, helped himself to my new umbrella as he went through the hall ? However, supposing that I did marry to please you and to give you an interest in life, and that the marriage proved to be a failure?"</p>
<p>"It could not be ! "--with much decision.</p>
<p>"No; such things never occur ! "</p>
<p>"Not with the sort of girl I have in my mind ; and you,
<pb n="27     A YOUNG MAN's FANCY."/>
my dear boy, have the best heart and sweetest temper in the ,sorld. I don't want to flatter you but--"
<p>"But you are flattering me absurdly," he interrupted. "I have by no means a sweet temper. Just ask my bearer I "</p>
<p>"Well, well "&#x2014;impatiently&#x2014;'4 then call it whatever you please. Of one thing I am certain&#x2014;you would be extremely happy ; and she has fifteen hundred a year of her own."</p>

<p>"Miss Nancy Budd," rejoined Mrs. Romilly, triumphantly.</p>
<p>"No, mother ; not if she had fifteen hundred thousand pounds I "</p>

<p>"Would they not ? thought Mrs. Romilly, with a quick dance at her son. He, was not much above middle height, «ud had a slight, well -knit figure ; his close-cropped brown hair exhibited 'a wave inherited from her own frizzy locks ; his face was tanned to mahogany, but to strangers&#x2014;much less his mother&#x2014;it was a handsome face, with its clear-cut, features and sweeping auburn moustache ; and even by men '~"'0 are never prone to admire one another&#x2014;Gerald.

<p>" Well, Gerald," she began, of ter a moment's silence, "tell me seriously what you think about getting married."</p>

<p>" Who is she ? " she asked in aflutter of curiosity. " Tell me all about her."</p>
<p>" I have seen one girl I could have married, whom I could have adored ; but we never, never can be anything to one another ; I may not even speak to her ! "</p>
<p>"Oh, my dear boy," in a shocked, voice, "what are you going to tell me ? She is not a married woman ? "</p><p> " Well, not exactly."</p>
<p>" Where does she live ? Where have you seen her?"</p>
<p>" I have seen her, and so have you, in Dirs. Carwithen's
drawing-room," and then in a mysterious whisper he added,
"hanging on the wall."</p>
<p>" Hanging on the wall ! " echoed Mrs. Romilly, and her face betrayed sincere perplexity.</p>
<p>"Yes, Lady Juliet, lovely Lady Juliet ; the girl in the black hat."</p>
<p>"Of course you turn everything into a joke," cried Mrs. Romilly, in a tone of intense exasperation. " And I see nothing in the picture ; nothing but a woman with a small

<pb n="29     A YOUNG MAN'S FANCY."/>
face, a pair of roguish eyes, and quantities of red hair, a
colour I detest."
<p>Oh, mother, you have done it now, and wounded me in
my most sensitive point. Pray what do you call my moustache ? However, I forgive you this time, if you will please bear in mind that I admire red hair, and if in your travels you ever come across a girl with a face like Lady Juliet's, you are to take her home at once, and wire for me, for you ,%-ill have found your daughter-in-law at last."</p>
<p>"Which is as much as saying you will never marry ! cried Mrs. Romilly, impatiently. "I dare say you think I am a horrible mercenary old match-maker ; but you know very well, Jerry, that I would snatch at any straw to keep you with me. However, I have one consolation " and she drew herself up slightly.</p>
<p>" And what is that ? that you are always first with me, and that you know very well that if you really insist on it, I will remain at home, and chuck the service."</p>
<p>"No, Jerry ; after all, I could not be so selfish. I can see your side of the question ; " and then she added with a little si-h, " and perhaps one idle man at home is enough. My consolation is, that being so fastidious, and so invulnerable, there is no fear of your entangling yourself with some wretched girl out in India."</p>
<p>" No fear, indeed," emphatically. « And here comes Dolly Carwithen, arm-in-arm with Lord Hougham. Yes, happy Dolly ; just as he likes to be seen. I wonder what has brought him over to these parts ? What do I behold ? He is actually leaving the earl, and I believe he is coming to
speak to us !"</p><p>Gerald did not care for Mr. Carwithen ; his keen eyes detected flaws in this brilliant specimen of humanity. He figuratively held this ornament of society between his eye and the light, and found him to be paste. Mr. Carwithen was an ineffable dandy&#x2014;that was a pardonable weakness ; but he was a disgusting toady, and always running after big-wigs, and this was a failing for which this bronze-faced,
hawk-eyed young man felt the most measureless contempt.</p>
<p>On his side, Dolly Carwithen did not like ours Romilly;
he had a sharp tounge and a merry, mocking eye, and not a 
<pb n="30     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
grain of deference in his manner, for a man who was rich who was familiarly slapped on the back by at least half a dozen peers, and who gave the best little dinners in Dover.
<p>They avoided one another by mutual consent, and with considerable success, and therefore Gerald Romilly was a good deal surprised to find himself accosted and drawn aside by Mr. Carwithen&#x2014;it was merely to ask some trivial ques. tion about the trains ; and this question answered, Dolly still remained in his vicinity, and finally suggested that they should take a little turn together&#x2014;this with his most war. ranted bonhonzie, an air specially reserved for people of a higher grade than a lieutenant in a Ghoorka regiment, Gerald could not but accede; his mother preferred remaining near the band. She had met some friends, and there was nothing to prevent his accepting Mr. Carwithen's best cheroot &#x2014;and his company.</p>
<p>After a few trifling observations, Dolly nodded towards the sea, and said, " I suppose you will soon be crossing, on your way out ? "</p>
<p>"Yes ; in about three weeks, my time will be up."</p>
<p>"Ah! and where is your regiment now ? You told me, but I forget."</p>
<p>"At Durano, in the Himalayas ; it is our head-quarters."</p>
<p>" I suppose you know the place pretty well, eh, and the people ? "&#x2014;with a swift glance out of the corner of his eye. As Mr. Carwithen made the inquiry, there was a slight but perceptible change in the expression of his face, a change from languid politeness to keen interest ; and in a second it flashed on his companion that he had thus drawn him aside in order to ask him some special question&#x2014;well aloof from other ears.</p>
<p>"Yes, I know it, I dare say, as well as you know Dover."</p><p> "'What sort of a billet is it ? " continued Mr. Carwithen, carelessly.</p>
<p>" Oh, a capital station, but not a bit fashionable ; there is mahseer fishing, there is chikor and karkar shooting, but not much else, unless you are a first-class athlete, and can climb like a goat."</p>
<p>" Do you know the neighbourhood?"</p>
<p>"Yes, I know the neighbourhood," repeated Romilly.</p>
<pb n="31     A YOUNG MAN'S FANCY."/>

<p>"Not much society, I suppose? Not many spins., ch ?"</p>
<p>"No ; mostly married people, indifferently.
Have you ever come across a couple of the name of--of Noote?" And again there was a that furtive glane out of the corner of his eye.</p>
<p>"Noote ? No, never," and he laughed. " I am sure, if I
had, I should remember the name."</p>
<p>"Are you quite certain ? "--with ill-disguised anxiety.</p>
<p>"Yes ; at any rate, they are not in the station ; but there are lots of missionaries and ;planters and retired officers scattered over the hills within a radius of forty miles."</p>
<p>"Oh, I see ; and they never show up ? "</p>
<p>"No, very rarely. They live like hermits among their gardens and their flocks and herds.</p>
<p>"A frightful existence," said Carwithen, shrugging his shoulders	it would kill me in a week."</p>
<p>I dare say it would ; and regarding these Nootes, I can make inquiries about them, if you like. Shall I look them up for you ?</p>
<p>"On no account, no account, my dear fellow," rejoined the other
precipitately. " I merely asked because I came across them once in a
small matter of business, and&#x2014;avid that's all&#x2014;they don't
interest me in the least. Ah, I see Lord lIougham is looking for me
everywhere ; he came over with me, you know&#x2014;is dining with us
this evening&#x2014;such a charming fellow&#x2014;introduce you to him
another time. Ah, good day and he hurried off.</p>
<milestone unit="section" rend="stars"/>
<p>The Carwithens were wealthy people&#x2014;that is to say, he had a few hundreds per annum, and she had many thousands ; but, then, he possessed birth and connections, whilst she well, the truth must be disclosed, Rumour for once was right &#x2014; siie Avas the daughter of an obscure money-lender ; at least his origin was obscure, though his trans-actions were quite the reverse. Having acquired a large fortune and a most unenviable reputation, he had departed this life, leaving his ill-gotten riches to his only child, Miss Lydia Ganimick, a personable spinster of twenty-six, with a goitionod figure, a strong will, and a large fund of social ambition. Miss Gaznmick resided on the Continent for
<pb n="32     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
the first year after her bereavement, and had a considerable number of worshippers, but among them all she kept her head cool. Birth for bullion was her bargain, and she was as fully resolved to have the worth of her money as might
be expected from her father's daughter. She had the peerage at her fingers' ends, and was prepared to marry any one above the rank of a knight. Nevertheless, in the end, she gave her hand&#x2014;and an iron one he found it&#x2014;to good-looking, graceless Dolly Carwithen. True, his family was ancient, his second cousin was a peer, but his enemies and rivals hinted that there was some story about him in India, that he was in debt, and that he had been hard at work heiress-hunting for the last three years. ilTiss Gammick listened demurely to all these tales&#x2014;listened and was lost to her herds of adorers ; for she married Dolly Carwithen. They had now been husband and wife for fifteen years, and on the whole the alliance had turned out well for both parties. For a poor man, what can be an easier or more agreeable form of livelihood than a wealthy wife who worships him ? And, on the other hand, the low-born Miss Gammick was well satisfied with her matri--monial venture. ^Her husband's relatives tolerated her, she moved in good society, her card-plate exhibited the names of great ladies, and she had been presented at court. She was most enthusiastic about Dolly's ancestors (having none of her own), and discovered many curious facts respecting them, which she brought to light for the first time, to the amazement of the family. A Carwithen had been second cousin once removed to Queen Boadicea, and been a conspicuous figure in the sacking of London ; a Carwithen had been one of the seven champions of Christendom ; a Carwithen had placed the historical chair for King Canute ; and a Carwithen had taken part in every one of the eight crusades&#x2014;not even excepting the children's futile expedition.
<p>Mrs. Carwithen had also a wonderful collection of so-called heirlooms, very valuable possessions, having cost a round sum of money. But Lady Juliet was no impostor, she was a bond fide ancestress, and had been fished out of a lumber-room in Doily's old home, covered with dust and

Mrs. Carwithen had also a wonderful collection of so-called heirlooms, very valuable possessions, having cost a round sum of money. But Lady Juliet was no impostor, she was a bond fide ancestress, and had been fished out of a lumber-room in Doily's old home, covered with dust and
<pb n="34     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
returned fourfold ;&#x2014;he was always most attentive to her in public, and in private ; remembered her special tastes, her favourite flowers, her birthday, and, above all, he allowed her to open his letters ! The Carwithens had a flat in London as a pied-a-terre, and roamed about the greater part of the year&#x2014;to the baths abroad, to Cowes, to Scotland, At present they had rented a furnished house in Dover, where they entertained, and gave first-rate dinners to really important social creditors ; far be it from them to open their doors to passing acquaintances&#x2014;as did the benighted Romillys, indifferent to clothes, to pedigree, to all but the memories of auld lang syne.
<p>Mr. Carwithen, when he examined his mind, frankly confessed that he had done well for himself in marrying Lyddy Gammick ; he had lea a luxurious and fairly successful existence since he had made her his bride ; he enjoyed a fine income, without the exertion of earning it; he had nothing to do but gratify his tastes and likings. Iie liked his clubs ; he liked to be admirably turned out, and to pass for a man of five and thirty ; he liked a good dinner ; he liked writing letters from grand country places and castles&#x2014;with the address printed on the note-paper--to less fortunate mortals. On the whole, his likings were gratified ; good looking, the husband of a devoted partner, with no bills, no malady, no encumbrance, and no greater anxiety than slight misgivings as to the competence of his cook or his tailor.</p>
<p>And yet he was in a similar position to the princess of whom we have read ; there was one little pea beneath the pile of forty down feather-beds, that interfered with all his repose.</p>

  <div id="005" type="chapter" n="5"><head>CHAPTER V.<lb/>
DOLLY'S SECRET.</head>
"Pluck the heart out of my mystery." <lb/>Hamlet.
It was quite true that Dolly Carwithen had a secret in his life&#x2014;a secret connected with India ; and, as there is no occasion to conceal it, pending Mrs. Mander s's return, it shall be divulged without delay.
<p>One very wet August afternoon, nineteen years ago, a small procession might have been seen winding its way up the steep bridle-path that leads from the Brewery to Chotalz Bilat. It was the last and worst burst of the monsoon, and the rain was coming down like a waterspout. The small mountain torrents were brown rushing rivers, boiling over with dirty white foam. The low country was enveloped in a grey mist. It was not a promising day, and nothing but the most urgent private affairs would be likely to summon any sane person from beneath the noisy shelter of their zinc roof. All up and down the usually thronged bridle-road there was not a soul to be seen, save this little party who were making their way in the very teeth of the weather : headed by a handsome young man of four or five and twenty &#x2014;in fact, Dolly Carwithen®-looking hollow-eyed, haggard, and depressed. He seemed to be buried in his reflections, and totally indifferent to the miniature cataract that was streamino, off his mackintosh, down the sides of his panting
hill pony, and trickling out of the toes of his boots. His
Terai hat was reduced to a mere pulp. Truly he resembled the traditional drowned rat, as he and his steed at last turned into the bazar road. Here was no view awaiting them of a lovely lake, reflecting surrounding hills, as in a mirror--no nothing but sheets of impenetrable fog and mountains shrouded in sullen-looking clouds. The bazar was empty, save for a few shivering pack ponies, waiting miserably under dripping verandahs, whilst their owners
<pb n="36     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
squatted within, drinking smoke from the inevitable huka, and listening to all the local gup.
<p>At the corner of the bridge Mr. Carwithen halted and looked behind him, and the dejected expression of his countenance gave way to one of angry impatience.</p>
<p>" What the mischief is keeping them? " he muttered. " It will be dark before we get there. I'd like to thrash the life out of those devils of coolies!"</p>
<p>Ere he had concluded this benevolent wish, the remainder of the procession turned the corner. First, a stout hill-man, with enormous shoulders and brawny limbs, carrying a bog upon his back, his person enveloped in a coarse brown blanket, fastened across the chest with rude brass pins. Next came a "dandy," a sort of chair, borne by four other coolies ; in this sat a benumbed ayab, with an umbrella over her head, and a sleeping infant in her lap.</p>
<p>" Chullo I Chullo I " shouted the young man impatiently. " Come, get on. ahead of me, you lazy brutes."</p>
<p>And he proceeded to herd the party up a narrow, slippery path that led to a mall overlooking the lake. After following the mall for some time, under dripping trees, and sponge-like overhanging ferns, they came in sight of a large grey house, with a high zinc roof--a severe-looking residence, and it did not need the gravelled playground or the board outside the gate, on which was painted " Miss Bruce's Establishment for Young Ladies," for "School " was written all over it in capital letters.</p>
<p>There was no straggling flower-garden, no waving creepers, weedy walks, or barking dogs&#x2014;everything was plain and neat and simple, from the bright windows and white painted swing to the stern old bearer who invited the new arrivals into the verandah.</p>
<p>Dolly Carwithen took off his soaking hat and coat ; the ayah disentangled herself and Miss Bruce's new young lady from their wraps ; the coolies and pony got under shelter when Miss Bruce herself came on the scene&#x2014;a short, spare lady of about thirty, neatly dressed in black, with black hair, black eyes, a very long nose, a very long chin, and a tightly closed mouth&#x2014;by no means a beautiful face, but the face of a clever and determined woman.</p>
<pb n="37     DOLLY'S SECRET."/>
<p>"I scarcely expected you this dreadful day," she said in a cheery voice, as she offered her hand to Dolly, much surprised to find that this good-looking young man was the paterfamilias who had been in correspondence with her. He Ivas of such a widely different type to the parents of her other
charges.</p>
<p>
Miss Bruce, an accomplished and enterprising English governess, had !come out to India a few years previously with the idea that a well-managed exclusive girls' school, if situated in the hills, would pay. It would be a new experiment, and she was excessively sanguine of its results. She rented a large house, added a schoolroom, furnished the premises, laid in three second-hand pianos, secured two efficient assistants, and confidently awaited the result. But alas for her hopes, for her friends' fine promises, and her own circulars, officers and civilians still continued to send their daughters home, just as if " St. Oswald's " was not in existence, or ready to supply what Miss Bruce declared must be a long-felt want. She was accordingly advised to moderate her expectations, and to what our American cousins call " climb down "&#x2014;in other words, to open her arms to the children of Eurasians and tradespeople&#x2014;children whose parents were willing and able to afford a good education in a good climate. The change had answered extremely well. The school numbered fifty boarders&#x2014;punctually paid for&#x2014;and who possibly gave less anxiety and trouble than those delicately reared liot-house plants&#x2014;daughters of colonels and collectors. Yes, the school was in a most flourishing condition, and Miss Dig, the head-governess and lady principal's right hand, was amazed to hear that they were about to receive an infant aged but two months, and her amazement naturally found speech.</p>
<p>"Why?" echoed Miss Bruce. "I'll, tell you, Agnes. Firstly, because all is fish that comes into my net&#x2014;even this little minnow. Secondly, because sixty rupees a month, and her ayah paid for, is a consideration. The child won't cost ten. Thirdly, and lastly, because she is likely to be
permanent."</p>
<p>For all these reasons, and also by means of his handsome face and gallant bearing, Miss Bruce smiled graciously on
<pb n="38     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
Dolly Carwithen when she said, "I scarcely expected you to-day."
<p>" But you got my telegram	he inquired, as he dried
his damp face in his silk handkerchief.</p>
<p>" Oh yes. Won't you come in ? Oh, never mind your boots ! " she added recklessly, ushering him into a comfortable carpeted room, with tempting armchairs and a roaring wood fire, and beckoning to the ayah to follow them.</p>
<p>"This is my assistant, hiss Dix," she continued, as a pale, refined-looking woman, wrapped in a shawl, rose and bowed." Miss Dix will have a good deal to say to my little charge," now turning to examine the infant, who suddenly opened a pair of brown eyes, and having stared at the stranger for some seconds, wrinkled up its face, and gave vent to a succession of yells. Miss Bruce could not truth-f ully say, " What a lovely child ! what a little angel ! " for at present its nose was a button and its hair of a blazing red, so she merely exclaimed--</p>
<p>"Poor darling ! she must be cold and hungry," and, addressing her ayah in fluent Hindustani, sent her out of the room in company with Miss Dix, who, taking the child in her arms, limped away with her down the co ridor, Miss Dix had been lame from her birth.</p>
<p>You see, I had to come up to-day," explained the young man, seating himself. « I only got three days' leave ; the child is not a bit the worse."</p>
<p>"I hope not. You will have some refreshment, won't you ? A cup of hot coffee, or a peg, or anything ? "</p>
<p>Thanks, no ; I am going to the hotel. I can't stop long," he went on uneasily, with a glance at his wet boots. "And I go down first thing in the morning."</p>
<p>But, surely this young man, with a pair of somewhat faithless-looking blue eyes, was not going to leave the child and depart in this fashion, without one word on business ?</p>
<p>Miss Bruce's face assumed its gravest professional aspect, and her nose seemed to grow longer as she said--</p>
<p>" I see you are very wet, and it would be cruel to detain you now. But you call and make all arrangements to-morrow, before leaving the station ?"</p>
<pb n="39     DOLLY'S SECRET."/>
<p>"Arrangements ? " he echoed, with a blank look.</p>
<p>"Yes, of course, about the child," said the lady, who had a decisive disposition and prompt manner.</p>
<p>"Oh, aw," pulling his moustache. "Sixty rupees a month, is it not ? "</p>
<p>Yes, paid in advance, and you pay the ayah," said Miss Bruce, in her most practical tones.</p>
<p>"All right ; if you like."</p>
<p>"Why, you suggested it yourself I " she exclaimed, with a touch of impatience. " Am I to have entire charge of the child, and keep her here for her holidays, and provide her clothes ? "</p>
<p>" Yes, rather," with an emphasis that did not say much for his parental affection. "You see, I shall be with my regiment. I am going to a camp of exercise this autumn ; anyway, I can't be lugging a baby and an ayah about the world."</p>
<p>" Of course not. The clothes will be extra, and I must keep an additional cow ; and there will be wood for baths, and all that," resolved to drive a good bargain with this somewhat nonchalant young officer.</p>
 <p>"Oh, I say, I can't afford more than sixty rupees for everything. You see, she does not eat. She won't want lessons for ages. Airs. Warry said sixty was too much," lie added with boyish candour.</p>
<p>Miss Bruce made a mental note of Mrs. Warry's name, and said with affected resignation--</p>
<p>" Well, it is very little ; but I suppose I must try and mare it do I A young child is a great responsibility."</p>
<p>" Yes ; but she is as hard as nails. It's a bore she is so awfully ugly. Only for that, I'd have packed her off home &#x2014;like a shot."</p>
<p>"I wonder you did not send her to your own people."</p><p> "I'll tell you the reason," he rejoined, with an air of superficial frankness.</p>
<p>" My people don't know I am married. I may as well tell you, and make a clean breast of it, for some one else is sure to be good enough to do it for me. My wife was not in my own class of life--a barrack sergeant's daughter--and so, you see, I funked telling the old folks at home."</p>
<pb n="40     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
<p>" But surely they must know some day."</p>
<p>"Yes, some day ; if the child had been pretty, or like the Carwithens, I might have despatched it. But it's such 1a hideous little devil, they would pitch it into the street."</p>
<p>" I don't think there would have been any risk of that," rejoined Miss Bruce. " What is her name ? "</p>
<p>" Juliet&#x2014;an old family one&#x2014;but you may call her Julia Car&#x2014;the other is such a mouthful, and Car is good enoucr L for school use."</p>
<p>"Nevertheless, I think she had better be known by her full name," said Miss Bruce, stiffly ; "otherwise there might be awkward complications."</p>
<p>" Very well, it's all one to me. No, by Jove, on second thoughts, it is not. Carwithen is not a common name, people might ask questions ; for the present she is to be Car only. She can take the rest of her name within a year or so. See the joke, eh ? "</p>
<p>" No ; my sense of humour is somewhat deficient," said Miss Bruce, stiffly.</p>
<p>"Oh, come. I suppose I may call my own property what I please ? " he exclaimed, rising. « I'll ride up to-morrow, and bring you the coin ; it's with my baggage. I'm going to leave the child with you for a long time, you know."</p>
<p>"Yes, I quite understand that, and I will do my very best for her, you may be certain. I'll make her my special charge."</p>
<p>"Yes ; but you'll never make her a beauty. Now I must be off."</p>
<p>Shaking hands with alacrity, and seemingly thankful to have disposed of a troublesome business, he hurried out, pulled on his moist mackintosh, mounted his pony, and galloped away.</p>
<p>" What a harum-scarum creature, and how handsome lie is I " mentally exclaimed Miss Bruce. " Fancy his telling vie all his family affairs. He has forgotten to pay the coolies. Well, I am not going to do it for him," and, opening the window, she shrieked out a sentence that sent them all streaming after him. Then she went upstairs, and looked critically at her new boarder. She was sound asleep on the ayah's lap, and, really, not at all so plain, only for
<pb n="41     DOLLY'S SECRET."/>
her hair, which was very abundant and very red. She had dark eyelashes and a lovely skin. Poor mite ! What would be her fate, in a world where she was evidently not wanted ?
<p>Then Miss Bruce proceeded to judiciously cross-examine the ayah. " How long had she been with the mem sahib ? "</p>
<p>"One year&#x2014;very pretty lady&#x2014;dark eyes&#x2014;dark hair--and so young when she died. Sahib very sorry for two three days."</p>
<p>"Sorry for two or three days ! " thought Miss Bruce. "What an eloquent sermon on matrimony ! After all, I believe a single woman, like myself, has far the best of it. I won't have the child's box unpacked until to-morrow, and until I see if that young man means to pay. Supposing he was to go down, and never come near me ! It's rather a shame to doubt him, but these gay and reckless youths are sometimes very careless about money. If he plays me false, I shall just send the child to the dak bungalow. I cannot afford to support strangers' children."</p>
<p>During that night, had Miss Bruce but known it, little Juliet's destination hung by a hair.</p>
<p>Dolly had gone to a popular hotel, and there, warmed, refreshed, and reclothed, had presented himself in the card-room, and been welcomed with acclamation by several acquaintances, who were glad to see any new face that dreary day, and to the query, " Hullo, Doll, what brought you up here he simply shrugged his shoulders, laughed, and said, "A bazar pony."</p>
<p>He had not intended to play cards&#x2014;had merely come to look on&#x2014;for he had barely sufficient cash to pay three months in advance and his return journey. But he was as weak as water where cards were concerned, and where his own pleasure was in question, and he was easily persuaded to cut in. Rubber followed rubber, and, alas ! his rupees soon began to melt like snow before the fire ; rupee points and a gold mohur on the rub, and bets, soon runs away with a considerable sum, and presently Dolly was cleared out. Luck was dead against him. After dinner, and fortified by a bottle of champagne, he went to the Club Baboo, and borrowed one hundred rupees on his watch and chain, and
<pb n="42     A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
once more sat down to tempt Fortune, and Fortune fiburatively threw herself into his arms.
<p>" Carwithen's luck " that night was talked of for two seasons 1 Ile held extraordinary cards&#x2014;honours, trumps, aces, kings seemed to tumble out of his hands at his will; he betted audaciously, he backed himself heavily ; it was all the same, he could not lose ! He played till three o'clock in the morning. Who would believe that this haggard, fierce-eyed young gambler, with wild hair and unsteady hand, could ever develop into staid, self-contained, artificial Adolphus Carwithen ? He rose at last, the winner of four thousand rupees, and, with his pockets stuffed with notes, cheques, and I O U's, he staggered off to bed, literally drunk with success l It was late when he awoke, and as lie dressed and ate his breakfast he came to an unusually prudent resolution. Ile would pay over half his winnings to Miss Bruce there and then, nearly four years in advance, and then he need not bother his head about the child for the whole of that period. She would be provided for, at no cost to him ; no&#x2014;and he chuckled at the thought&#x2014;the price of her maintenance had all come out of the pockets of Algy White, Skippy Byng, and Bobby I.angstaffe. IIe would keep the rest to pay some of his debts, and to buy a couple of ponies -for the Umballa races. Yes, he would make over the coin to Miss Bruce at once ; strike whilst the iron was hot, and ere lie changed his mind&#x2014;he so often changed his mind. Ile sorted out notes and cheques from the I O U's, and put them in a large envelope, and, after settling his bill, set off on his return journey, laden with his spoils. No ; he was not going to stop another night to give his friends their revenge, although they demanded it clamorously ; his leave was up, and lie was off. He was deaf to all entreaties and blandishments, and his disappointed companions angrily remarked " that it was the first time they had ever known Carwithen so keen about returning to his duty ! "</p>
<p>Miss Bruce was delighted to see him, and repented her of her base suspicions ; and her surprise was unbounded when Dolly placed a thick packet of notes in her hands, and said, with his most offhand air&#x2014;</p>
<pb n ="43     MR. CARWITHEN'S PAST."/>
"Here's two thousand rupees, payment in advance. Please see that it's all square, for I have not a minute to
lose."</p>
<p>"But, my dear Mr. Carwithen, I don't want all thus," she
protested. " Six months--"</p>
<p>"Keep it now you have got it&#x2014;that is my advice to you," he interrupted impatiently as he picked up his whip.</p>
"But supposing anything were to happen, and the child were&#x2014;Were to die ? "
<p>"No such " lie pulled himself up sharply. "No fear of that. Just see that it's all right, please, for I must be going."</p>
<p>Miss Bruce hastily counted it over, and said, "Yes, I believe it's correct. So I will send you a formal stamped receipt&#x2014;or can you wait now ? Wait five minutes, and see the child, and take a receipt."</p>
<p>"No, not a second, or I'll miss my dAk. I shall have to gallop all the way to catch it, as it is. However, I can always do wonders with my own spurs and another fellow's nag," and with a hasty shake of the hand he was gone. Within three weeks, every single rupee of his winnings was also gone&#x2014;not in paying bills&#x2014;lost on a racecourse, and he said to himself, as he thrust his hands into his now empty pockets, "What a comfort it is that for once in my life I had some sense, ands paid up Miss Bruce like a nlan&#x2014;ay, like a nobleman 1 The rest of that coin would have gone the same way, and I am still that to the good."</p></p></p></p></div> <div id="006" type="chapter" n="6"><head>
CHAPTER VI.<lb/>
MR. CARWITHEN'S PAST.<lb/>


" A man of pleasure is a man of pains."<lb/>
