<p>"I cannot rightly say now ; they are all dead. Old Smithson used to brag of his family ; but that was all my eye. He was a gin-drinking, beer-swilling old rascal ; and you see, between him on one side, and your father on the other, you've no call to be <hi rend="italic">proud</hi>."</p>
<p>She looked at the girl walking beside her, with her clear-cut features and delicate, half-scornful grace, her slender figure, her swift, elastic tread, and secretly wondered how she came to be so different to any one she had ever seen--so different to Lottie Smithson, who could not walk a yard, who was always complaining of heat, or cold, or exertion, and whose tears were ever near her eyes and her feelings on
<pb n="108 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>

the surface. Juliet Carr was uncommon in every way ; she never complained, she never wept, she kept her thoughts to herself&#x2014;too much to herself&#x2014;and even now, after having been distinctly shown her place in society, and been&#x2014;;Mrs. Noote flattered herself --taken down a peg, she was walking
along with her head as high as ever ! The only change in her usual appearance was a crimson spot on either check. Such an aristocratic-looking niece was a profitable social appendage ; people might ask her out now, but not one step should Juliet stir without her newly elected aunt.</p>
<p>" The idea," she broke out, " of the Cassons asking you over to spend the day ! And without <hi rend="italic">me</hi> ! as if I was not good enough company. Such impudence ! I just said to Mrs. Casson, as I came off now, that you never stirred a yard without me; and as to her coming over to us, as she offered, to have a chat with you, I said we had very little time for visitors. She won't invite herself over again in a hurry."</p>
<p>This was Juliet's last expedition into society for some time.</p>
<p>Winter came. The snow lay two feet on the ground ; long rambles were impracticable, and Jnliet was, like Mariana in the moated grange, " aweary, aweary," of hours of en-forced idleness, of hours of backgammon in an atmosphere reeking with tobacco, weary of Captain Noote's questions, and Mrs. Noote's nagging tongue. She spent most of her pocket-money on books and papers and postage stamps ; she wrote long letters to her school-fellows and Mrs. Radford ; but many of the eagerly expected replies Mrs. Noote poked into the middle of the kitchen fire. It gave her a secret thrill of pleasure to see the girl's face of blank disappointment when she merely handed her two or three papers and a post-card ; it was <hi rend="italic">her</hi> revenge for sonic of Juliet's sharp speeches, and nonchalant, disdainful ways.</p>
<p>Mrs. Noote complained to Mrs. Rozario that her niece had a wonderful taste for low company, a statement that the latter absolutely declined to believe.</p>
<p>" She is not <hi rend="italic">that</hi> sort," she protested indignantly.</p>
<p>" Maybe not, but she is always down in the village, poking about among the people there. She gives them quinine,
<pb n="109 MRS. NOOTE SURPRISES MRS. CASSON."/>
and dresses ra dolls, and makes jackets for the children, and they stuff her with stories about fairies and devils and teach her music. Why, she and Gindia, the gwala's wife, are as thick as thieves."</p>
Teach her music ! Oh well, I cannot get over that ! " gasped Lulalie, in a tone of horror.
<p>"No. And you should hear her playing on the sitar, and singing hill songs ; and when she passes through the village I declare; they salaam to her, as if she was a goddess, and never takes no notice whatever of me&#x2014;no more nor if I was so much dirt."</p>
<p>Mrs. Noote's arraignment was partly correct. Juliet spoke the Pahari tongue fluently ; she was in a way a hill girl herself, and when she went down to the village, on an errand to the Bunnia's shop, or on her own account, every one was glad to see her. First of all, she had made friends with the sturdy brown children, and then with their parents &#x2014;who were children too&#x2014;most of them high-caste Brahmins, whose simple dignity and singlemindedness and courtesy afforded a strong contrast to the inmates of Bala Dara. They all adored the bliss Sahib, whom they named among themselves « Sunduria," that is to say, " the beautiful," and gladly welcomed her under their flat stone roofs, and offered her dahlias and marigolds. She knew their family histories and ancient feuds, and all about their lawsuits, leases of land, and the condition of their cattle and crops. It was true that Gindia taught her how to play the sitar&#x2014;an instrument made out of a gourd with strings like a guitar&#x2014;and Gindia's old grandmother, Neoli, poured into her ears many legends and tragedies, and tales of self-immolation, and desperate pilgrimages to almost inaccessible mountain shrines. She gave her a smooth dark-green stone, pierced, to wear as an amulet against the evil eye, and cautioned her to beware of the big blind bull who haunted the Taka valley, or of the Dekkance woman, who cast spells near the old water mill, and above all of the mad fakir.</p>
<p>Juliet, on her side, bad many strange things to impart to wondering
listeners. She told them that the world was round ; she told them of
the great countries beyond the Eala Pani&#x2014;i.e. black water, or
sea ; she told them about
<pb n="110 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>

ships and steamers, and many things entirely new to them,
for these simple people had not had a college education, li ke the rising generation in the neighbourhood of Durano. All they knew of the great world was when their brothers, who were jampannis, who had been carrying gay mem sahibs all season, came home in winter, and counted out their piles of rupees in the midst of a transported circle, many of whom never handled a silver coin from one year to another. These adventurous relatives brought in a breezy whiff of outer atmosphere, and related wonderful tales of pony racing, and boating, and dancing, and relapsed in a few hours, from smart jampannis in blue-and-white livery, carrying notes or picking up tennis-balls, into common hill coolies in dirty skull caps and the universal brown blanket. These hill people were a contented race ; their interests were centred in a flock of goats, a good harvest of murga, and a few stony acres. They minded their cattle and their crops ; the rest was the will of God.</p>
<p>At last spring came&#x2014;welcome spring. The pine needles began to carpet the ground, the rhododendrons became masses of crimson, white, and even pink flowers. Shepherds might be met carrying tiny kids and lambs round their neck (after the manner of scriptural pictures), and Juliet commenced her long rambles once more. She feared no one&#x2014;nothing, not ever. the " I3hoots," against which she was so expressly cautioned, as, armed with a staff, and accompanied by Youchee, her dog, she explored far beyond their own valley ; meeting strings of soft-footed camels, or troops of flat-faced Tartars, coming in from Thibet, and spinning industriously as they walked in the rear of flocks of little long-haired goats, laden with borax and salt. She met droves of Bhoetia ponies, tied head and tail, and many pilgrims bound to the thrice holy shrines of Gangotri anti Badrinath ; but she never once encountered a European.</p>
<pb n="111 THE MOUNTAIN SPIRIT"/></p></div> <div id="015" type="chapter" n="15"><head>CHAPTER XV.<lb/>
THE MOUNTAIN SPIRIT.<lb/>
"When she had passed, it seemed like the ceasing of exquisite music."&#x2014;Evangeline, Part I.</head>
<p>PICTURE a Himalayan village at seven o'clock one bright dewy morning in early autumn, the long crooked street paved with irregular slabs, and bordered with low walls of rude blocks of stone. At either side flat-roofed houses, with elaborately carved wooden fronts, slant away among gardens of pumpkins, pepper, tall sunflowers, and tangles of single dahlias. To the rear are rows of primitive cowsheds. Houses, gardens, and cowsheds are alike half swallowed tip and lost to view among the high-standing yellow crops. In narrow doorways old men are sitting drowsily, smoking the
ever-soothing hookah, whilst their sons and daugliters are out gathering in the harvest ; children of tender years are leading whole brigades of sheep and cattle to their daily meagre pasture ; and two or three yelping long-legged pariahs appear to be left in sole charge of the juveuile population&#x2014;babies, kids, calves, and pony foals.</p>
<p>It is an ordinary Kumaoni hamlet, lying far remote from
big towns and cantonments, where the milking, reaping, weaving, and grinding, goes on with unbroken monotony year after year &#x2014; nay, century after century ; where a woman's brightest dream is to be possessor of the heaviest silver necklet ; a man's ambition is bounded by a pair of plough-bullocks ; and the only intelligence that ever penetrates from the outer world is that received from neighbour-in; villages, where -news is shouted from hillside to hillside, and thus flies through the country, literally by word of mouth.</p>
<p>Through such a village Gerald Pomilly was walking one early morning, with a gun upon his shoulder and a tune upon his lips. lie had left his parents still studying advertisements, and roaming England in search of a home, and had arrived in India a year previously ; he had spent the cold weather at a camp of exercise, and subsequently been sent on a survey trip to the frontier of Thibet, and had but recently returned
<pb n="112 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
to his accustomed haunts and accustomed duties. Now and then he obtained a few days' leave, and went off into tj.e wilds fishing and shooting far beyond the range of dak bungalows, merely camping in an eighty pound tent, and subsisting on the spoil of his rod cr gun. What happy hours were those, when he landed a fifty-pound mahseer from its home in a snow-fed river, or brought down a makor after half a day's hard stalking 1 He was out on one of these expeditious now, and on his way to a well-known beat, as he tramped through this little hamlet, whistling from pure light-heartedness and the exhilarating effects of this exquisite autumn morning.</p>
<p>In turning a sharp angle he was suddenly aware of a woman's approach, not a swarthy, flat-faced Kumaoni, with short, blue skirt, tanf;led locks, and reaping-hook, but&#x2014;Lady Juliet Carwithen, straight from her frame. He had almost forgotten the picture and his whimsical adoration, and here she came to remind him of her existence 1 Yes, he instantly recognized the bib hat, the white gown, the masses of red-brown hair, and the piquant face.</p>
<p>She carried a stick in her hand, and wore long wrinkled gloves, and the only difference from the painting lay in the fact that the stick was an alpenstock, and that she was accompanied by a large foolish-looking yellow dog.</p>
<p>The apparition met his bold gaze of amazement with a counter glance of haughty displeasure ; a slight pink tinge seemed to come into her cheek as she passed by, straight as an arrow, and light as the dew.</p>
<p>This was a strange time and place to see a good old English ghost&#x2014;a Kumaoni village at seven o'clock in the morning ! The appearance seemed to be full of vitality, and walked with a springy, not to say jaunty air, precisely the sort of gait he would have expected in Lady Juliet. He was too much astounded to shout or run after her ; he merely leant against the wall bordering the road, gazing and gazing, until the slight figure and broad hat turned round a curve leading through fields of yellow corn &#x2014;and so vanished. When she had disappeared he roused hinwself, and, hurrying to the nearest house, asked a sleepy patriarch, smoking in his doorway&#x2014;</p>
<pb n="113 THE MOUNTAIN SPIRIT"/>
<p>"Who was the Miss Sahib who had passed by ? "</p>
<p>The dreamy old person slowly removed his hookah, and shook his shorn head. He had not seen her.</p>
<p>To a child playing a jew's-harp, to a woman grinding corn, he put the same question, and received the same reply; and, indeed, she had passed so lightly, and drifted away so swiftly, that he could not wonder.</p>
<p>So he had seen a ghost--a young and lovely one, too&#x2014;one lie should like to see again. He did not divulge his adventure to a soul, but he dwelt upon the vision in the watches of the night. His friends remarked that he had not made half as good a bag as usual. Why ? Was game scarce ? How could he confess that he had not been looking for it, that he had been walking miles over the mountains in quest of another sort of quarry&#x2014;the ghost of a pretty girl ?</p>
<p>Captain Mackintosh, the brother officer who shared Mr. Romilly's
bungalow, was surprised when he asked him, with assumed carelessness,
if any English people lived near Kala Mutt.</p>
<p>" None that I ever heard of," replied his comrade, « except an old retired soldier who sells bacon and butter, and even he is miles from it."</p>
<p>"Has he any daughters ? " inquired the other, carelessly. "
</p><p>"No&#x2014;but stop, let me see. I think I heard the other
day that he had a pretty niece. Why do you ask ? "</p>
<p>"Mere idle curiosity," colouring to a deeper shade of tan.</p> <p>" And you are off to beat the same neighbourhood on
Thursday ? " exclaimed Captaiu Mackintosh, suspiciously.</p><p> " Yes, I think so."</p>
<p>" Although you had such wretched sport last time ; and you say that makor and even gurool have left the country ?"</p> "Yes," he assented doggedly.</div>
<p>" Gerald, come now, own up ; you have some other game on? "</p>
<p>"What sort of game ? " inquired Romilly, briefly.</p>
<p>" I am aware that you are not a lady's man ; but still, for all I know, you may have met your fate in the shape of some fair mountain spirit."</p>
<p>" If you think so, I invite you to come out and see the
<pb n="114 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
fair mountain spirit. You are not thinking of potheen, are you ? "</p>
<p>" No ; and you know very well that I loathe Irish whisky."</p>
<p>"Well, if you like to come this time, and don't mind roughing it, and engage to walk from twenty miles a day good, I'll indulge you with a bottle of the best Scotch."</p>
<p>But this invitation was emphatically declined. Gerald Romilly's game was possibly a myth, and certainly not worth the candle&#x2014;the candle being represented by Spartan fare, viz. black bread and spring water, rising before day-break, and following the steps of an active and intrepid sportsman. Roughing was all very well, but romilly cheer-fully endured downright hardship&#x2014;there was no fun in <hi rend="italic">that</hi> ; and Captain Mackintosh suffered him to depart in peace&#x2014;and alone.</p>
<p>He haunted the neighbourhood of Tula Mutt, he wandered round in a circuit of five miles, and saw nothing save croivs, goats, and buffaloes. At last he arrived at the conclusion that it must have been a trick of his imagination. What-ever it was, it was a curious experience, and an experience he was resolved to keep to himself. He had no desire to set the mess-table in a roar at his expense. How they would laul;h if he told them that he had met the original of an old portrait, walking down the street of an obscure Kumaoni village !</p>
<p>He still continued to go out shooting in quest of ordinary sport ; sometimes in company, but generally alone. Oiue afternoon, after a hard day's work, he missed his shikari, lost his way, and found himself close to a large, double-storied bungalow, and in a part of the hills to which he had never yet penetrated. He was in luck, for a terrible storm was brewing, the last word of the monsoon, and immense banks of ink-black clouds were drifting together, whit sheet lightning flickered round the mountains, and--
<q><l>"The wind, that grand old harper, </l><l>Swept his thunder-harp of pines."</l></q>
If the worst came to the worst, thought the sportsman, perhaps these people would put him up for the night ? lie
<pb n="115 FACE TO FACE."/>
approached the house by the back way, through wild tea-gardens, choked with white-headed grasses, and discovered tumbled-down stables, empty servants' quarters, and a moss-grown yard&#x2014;nothing to be seen on all sides but desolation and decay. The doors were closed ; so he made his way round to the front entrance. The door stood wide, and opened on a large hall and staircase ; but the premises appeared to be tenantless. He sat down upon the steps and lit (man's comforter) a pipe, and began to speculate as to his whereabouts, and how lie was to get home. Presently the rattle of the thunder grew louder and louder, and after a few solitary drops, the rain came roaring down upon the zinc-roofed verandah, as if the flood-gates of heaven had let loose a second deluge. Pomilly warmly congratulated him-self that he was under shelter, and just in the nick of time, as he listened to the deafening downpour above his head, and the sullen thunder reverberating among the mountains.</p>
<p>But stay ! surely there was another sound, caused neither by rain nor electricity&#x2014;a sound inside the house. It was a woman singing. He jumped to his feet, walked across the hall, seized the handle of a door to the left ; it opened into a large, low apartment, furnished as a drawing-room. Some one was sitting at the piano, who turned her head and abruptly ceased her song as he entered&#x2014;a girl, whose loveliness struck to his heart, who glowed, in the shadow of that diugv old room, like some brilliant flower or gem of colour. He was face to face with Lady Juliet.</p>
 <div id="016" type="chapter" n="16"><head>CHAPTER XVI.<lb/>
FACE TO FACE.<lb/>

" It is my lady : oh, it is my love !<lb/> Romeo and Juliet.</head>
<p>"LADY JULIET," he exclaimed involuntarily, as he advanced into the middle of the room, and took off his cap.</p>
<p>Juliet raised her eyes, and surveyed him as he approached and stood by the grand piano. Yes ; here was the same young man, with the bold grey eyes, who had stared at her
<pb n="116 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
so strangely when she passed him in Basita village some weeks before. She recognized him as one of a type she had seen at Chotah Bilat, loitering on the church steps, or galloping along the mall. He was an officer, and possibly from Durano. What had brought him to this part of the world ?</p>
<p>" I did not expect to see <hi rend="italic">you</hi> here," was his astounding remark.</p>
<p>I don't understand you," rejoined the young lady, in a clear but freezing tone&#x2014;a tone that, as he mentally re-marked, would have frozen a salamander&#x2014;a beautiful colour invaded her cheeks, and went and came with every breath, and there was a look of proud inquiry in her eyes. This, then, was no spirit from the other world, but a real live human being, who must be treated accordingly.</p>
<p>"I hope you will excuse my unceremonious entrance," said the stranger. " I took shelter from the rain, and had no idea the place was occupied. Will you give me house room till the storm is over ? "</p>
<p>" It is not my house," rejoined the lady, closing the piano as she spoke ; "you have as much right to be here as I have."</p>
<p>" Then you do not live here?"</p>
<p>"No; no one lives here, or has lived here for years. I come sometimes to practise on this old piano."</p>
<p>" I am very sorry I have interrupted you," he said. " I wish you would go on singing--Will you ? "
<p>" No, thank you, I had quite finished," rising and walking away from the instrument, and seating herself in a high-backed chair, looking&#x2014;had she but known it&#x2014;more like lady Juliet than ever.</p>
<p>" I must introduce myself. My name is Romilly."</p><p> Lady Juliet bowed&#x2014;a north-pole bow.</p>
<p>" I am quartered in Durano. You know Durano, of course ? "</p>
<p>"Very slightly."</p>
<p>Gerald Romilly, for once in his life, was actually shy and embarrassed&#x2014;his glib tongue faltered&#x2014;his merry glance was sobered ; in the company of this shabbily clad girl, sitting among dingy surroundings, he was less assured of
<pb n="117 FACE TO FACE."/>
himself, and more abashed, than he had ever been in the presence of royalty.</p>
<p>He ventured to draw up an old armchair at a respectful distance from the round table, seated himself, and, with one swift comprehensive glance, took in the situation. A long, low apartment, with three narrow windows, looking into a deep verandah. It was a damp room&#x2014;judging by the walls ; paper had peeled off in strips, and been nailed up again by a prentice hand ; the carpet was ancient and gaudy, the pattern red roses, the size of cabbages, connected by enormous streamers of blue ribbon ; the sofas and tables were those of a bygone day, the grand piano seemed to totter on its legs, its keys were yellow with age ; a few weather-stained prints hung on the walls, and some stiff wool-work screens and foot-stools testified to the industry of a former generation. Everything was old-fashioned, and out of date, and formed a fitting setting for Lady Juliet, as she sat witli.her hands folded in her lap, and her gaze intently fixed upon the torrents of rain that were streaming from the roof of the verandah. She was apparently as indifferent to Gerald Romilly's presence as if he were one of the faded prints upon the wall. Who could she be ? He asked himself. IIow came the living image of an old family portrait to be sitting; in this deserted bungalow, among the Kumaon hills ? Was she a ghost, after all'? She turned her face from its steady contemplation of the weather, their eyes met, and she blushed. He had never heard of a blushing ghost.</p>
<p>" Needless to ask <hi rend="italic">your</hi> name," he remarked with a genial smile I know you very well by sight. I have often seen you in Dover."</p>
<p>" I never was in Dover in my life," was the somewhat haughty answer.</p>
<p>"No ! but surely your name is Carwithen&#x2014;Juliet Car-withen?"</p>
<p>She made no answer, but looked at him in astonished silence.</p>
<p>" Is it not?"</p> he persisted.
<p>She made a faint affirmative movement of her head. </p><p>" How did you know ? " she presently asked in a low voice.</p><p> " I will tell you. When I was at home last year on leave,
<pb n="118 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
I knew a Mr. and Mrs. Carwithen in Dover, and in their drawing-room hangs a half-length picture of you."</p>
<p>" Of me ? Impossible ! I have never even been photo-graphed."</p>
<p>"At any rate, it is a portrait of Lady Juliet Carwithen, and might have been painted for you. I believe it is more than a hundred years old."</p>
<p>"And," struggling to speak gravely, "do you think that I am more than a hundred years old ? "</p>
<p>"No ; but for the life of me, I can't understand. You are her image ; you might have walked ont of the frame. You wear the very same sort of hat and dress."</p>
<p>You seem to have taken an interest in the picture."</p>
<p>Yes ; I used to be fearfully chaffed about it--" He stopped abruptly.</p>
<p>"Why ? " she demanded, transfixing him with a pair of beautiful eyes.</p>
<p>" Oh&#x2014;well&#x2014;I&#x2014;I can't exactly tell you." No ; it was not possible to confess to this somewhat austere young lady that it was because his insane admiration of the picture was a jest and a byword, and that actually a photograph of it had been pressed upon him by Mrs. Carwithen as a parting gift.</p>
<p>"Do you take me for a ghost ? " continued the living copy of the picture. "Perhaps you don't know that this is a haunted house, and that no one will stay here after dark but myself."</p>
<p>Yes ; she had the same mischievous look in her eyes as the portrait ; and lie was drifting into love, faster than ever autumn leaf drifted on a flush October river.</p>
<p>I must confess that I thought you were a ghost, when I met you in the village some weeks ago. It seemed strange to come across you in these wild regions. You do not live here, you say ? " repeating this question for the second time.</p>
<p>"No; this old house has not been inhabited for years ; it belonged, or belongs, to a General Trafford, and must have been a delightful spot once. You can see the remains of the orchards and gardens and terraces. There is quite a wilderness of lovely flowers ; the roses and heliotrope grown into enormous bushes ; and the tuberoses and jonquils are a
<pb n="119 FACE TO FACE."/>
sight. The old chokidar allows me to pick them, and to ramble about the house, and to play the piano, and to dust this room."</p>
<p>"Why is it deserted ? It is in a grand situation, and I noticed quantities of tea,&#x2014;and a tea-house, and coolie lines, as I came over the hills."</p>
<p>"There was cholera in the neighbourhood, and three of the Traffords died in one day ; they are buried under a deodar in the garden ; and then General Trafford shut up the house, and left the place almost as it stood, and has never been near it since. At first, his affents used to let it for the summer ; but it is too out of the way, and in too bad repair. The roof is in a dreadful state, and some of the floors upstairs are not safe, and it has a bad naine anion; the natives ; they call it the 'Devil's Bungalow.' "</p>
<p>"And you come here by yourself ?"</p>
<p>"Oh yes ; I am not the least superstitious. I come over about three times a week."</p>
<p>"Do you come from a distance ?" he asked craftily. About three miles. I live with people of the name of Noote."</p>
<p>" Noote ! " he gave an involuntary start. Yes ; lie remembered the name, and Mr. Carwithen's sly inquiries.</p>
<p>" Do you know Mr. Carwithen, who lives in Dover ? " he continued.</p>
<p>" No," dropped from her pretty lips like an icicle.</p><p> " Have you never seen him ? " he persisted.</p>
<p>" No "&#x2014;a pause. "When did <hi rend="italic">you</hi> see him last?"</p>
<p>"Let me think. I believe he came to see me off when I started for India."</p>
<p>"Then I conclude that he is a great friend of yours," and she turned and looked at him keenly.</p>
<p>"Seeing people off is not always a proof of attachment," he answered with a smile. "As you do not know him, there is no harm in my telling you that there is not much love lost between me and Mr. Carwithen."</p>

<p>" I believe he thinks that I am wanting in proper respect for Adolphus Carwithen, Esquire ; and I dislike him because he is such a worldly old sinner, always turning over his
<pb n="120 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
bread to see on which side is the butter, and running of the big-wigs--"</p>

<p>"Your real name is far prettier, and I shall always call you by it."</p>
<p>Juliet made no direct reply ; she was not likely to see this young man again, so what did it matter.</p>

<pb n="121 FACE TO FACE."/>

See those masses of clouds drifting slowly towards one another ; when <hi rend="italic">they</hi> meet, there will be a grand crash ; and look at the lightning playing round the mountains &#x2014;are you afraid ? "</p>

<p>He was leaning against a pillar, looping down on his new acquaintance, and in the fuller light of the verandah he was realizing how lovely she was. As he gazed, he marvelled at the tenacity of type in some races. Here was Lady Juliet revived in the person of her descendant. Had the original Lady Juliet such a wealth of hair ? such gold splashes among the bronze ? Had she as clear a complexion, as finely pencilled eyebrows as her great-great-granddaughter ? Pooh ! not she ! She was a fashionable eighteenth-century beauty, made up with white lead and red paint, and probably half her hair was false ! thus cruelly wronging his former divinity. There was, at any rate, no deception about her exquisite prototype, who sat gazing dreamily at the rain, wrapt in her own thoughts, and apparently oblivious of his presence. She was about nineteen years of age, and had lived in these hills all her life. How ? Why ? Where ? How came she by her English accent, her manners, her air of calm, unruffled repose ? Possibly the latter was inherited from the ancestress who had endowed her with her face and figure.</p>
<p>In spite of Juliet's appearance of tranquillity, her mind was in an uproar. Here was a man who had recently seen her father, who could tell her much about him and about his mode of life. How was she to question him ? He had confessed to their mutual dislike ; but this was an unparalleled opportunity. Time was precious ; such an occasion Ras not likely to fall to her lot twice.</p>
<pb n="122 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
<p>Thus it will be seen that each was anxious to cross. examine the other ; and the lady, as sometimes is the case, had the first as well as the last word !</p>
<p>" I know you do not like my father," she began abruptly. </p><p>" I would never have told you that, had I known who he was."</p>

an effort.</p>

<p>Whatever he was about to add was cut short in a violent manner, the storm which had been raging and raving, round the valley, making it resound as with a crack of musketry, and shaking the walls of the old bungalow at each peal, now culminated in one frightful crash of thunder directly over-head, the house seamed to rock to its foundations. As
<pb n="124 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>

So sudden and so close was the bolt, so blinding the flash, so stifling the sulphur, that Juliet could neither see nor speak ; she staggered blindly with her hands extended before her, and found herself received in some one's arms. Yes ; a man, a strange man, was holding her tightly. She felt the rough tweed coat against her cheek. She was conscious of a strong, re-assuring grasp about her waist ; the impropriety of the proceeding never even dawned upon her miud&#x2014;for it was entirely filled with the fear of death. In a very short time, half a minute, Juliet had come to her senses, coughed, opened her eyes, and quickly disengaged herself, still trembling violently.</p>
<p>"I thought I was struck blind," she gasped, half choked with the fumes of sulphur. " What an escape ! " and she shuddered.</p>
<p>" Yes, thank God ! " said her companion. " It was a near shave," pointing to the ploughed-up ground and blackened, smoking tree. " For once in our lives, we were within a few feet of death ; but the worst is over now&#x2014;two thunder-bolts never fall in the same place. I suppose this is the old chokidar ? He seems pretty well frightened out of his wits."</p>
<p>It was Krookia, the chokidar (or watchman) who lived on the premises, a tall old man in a dirty turban and a pea-green wadded coat, who now tottered into the verandah.</p>
<p>" It was to be looked for," he shouted. " There is a blight on the bungalow ; all the world knows it is the devil's house. The sahib and the Miss Sahib had better go, ere worse befall."</p>
<p>It will clear in ten minutes, and then we will go," said Gerald. "You can close the house as soon as you like&#x2014;don't mind us."</p>
<p>"Yes ; and a poor man that can never go, but must abide with these devils for six rupees a month ! " cried Krookia, passionately.</p>
<p>"It sounds a small sum, considering the society, but grain is cheap, and you can live on it comfortably. Why, you get
<pb n="125 FACE TO FACE."/>
twenty-five seers of barley for the rupee. You are as well off as any ghoorka in the lines."</p>
<p>"The sahib is joking. I can hardly exist. I swear it on the cow's tail, and this house is so lonely, so far from the bazaar. Does the sahib not want a chuprassi, or a chokidar, that he may lift his slave out of his misery ?"</p>
<p>"Why, Krookia, you told me one day that you had large property and lands," said Juliet.>/
<p>"Hazoor ! I did but jest. I have a share in my ancestral village, which is twenty koss from here ; the share is but seven rupees. You ask the people of my pergunnah ; my brother is rich, he owns ponies and takes road contracts, but Krookia is a poor, poor man."</p>
<p>Krookia had recovered his wits, got over his fright, and his two speaking eyes fixed on the sahib, saying, " Bucksheesh, bucksheesh," and 'again, " bucksheesh."</p>
<p>"How come you to be twenty koss from your own village ?" inquired Gerald, authoritatively.</p>
<p>" Lord of the world ! I was servant here, in the General Sahib's time. I was khitmaghar, when many sahibs and Miss Sahibs were here."</p>
<p>"Then you were here when they died?" interrupted Juliet, " those two girls who are buried in the garden."</p>
<p>" Alas ! I was here then. I saw the two sisters carried forth in the same hour ; one of them was beautiful, as it might be <hi rend="italic">you</hi>, Miss Sahib ; and a sahib in a pultoon (regi-ment), as it might be <hi rend="italic">this</hi> sahib, loved her. When I saw you and the sahib in the verandah just now, it brought the old days back." He blinked on them benignly, and Juliet became scarlet. "But she is dead," proceeded Krookia ; "withered in one day, like a flower, and the sahib got another wife. It is ever thus with young men : they love, and they forget ; except &#x2014;suddenly pulling himself up&#x2014;"this sahib here. It is written on his forehead that he would never forget."</p>
<p>"I never shall forget such an arrant old humbug," remarked Gerald ; and then catching sight of Juliet's face of cold displeasure, he hastened to change the subject, and exclaimed--</p>
<p>" You see, I am a true weather-prophet. Look ! it is going to clear, after all."</p> </p></p></p></p></p></div>
<pb n="126 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
<div id="017" type="chapter" n="17"><head>CHAPTER XVII.<lb/>
AFTER THE STORM.<lb/>
"But to be young was very heaven."</head>
<p>BY six o'clock it was a lovely evening. The downpour had ceased, the storm passed off, and with one of those swift changes common to the Himalayas, the clouds had dispersed, and the snows stood out with that dazzling brilliancy peculiar to breaks in the rains. A few heavy drops falling sullenly from the roof of the verandah, a few little trickling rivulets of tawny water scoring the gravel-drive, a few roses beaten to the earth, and the blackened remains of a white laburnum tree were all that testified to Nature's recent out-break. No one would believe, looking across the valley, now so green and brilliant, that it had so lately resembled the mouth of the bottomless pit, and had been filled with clouds and lightnin;, and, as it were, flames and smoke.</p>
<p>"It is quite fine now," said Juliet, drawing on her gloves and taking her alpenstock, "and I must go."</p>
<p>" You will allow me to accompany you home ? "</p>
<p>" Oh no. Pray don't think of it. I am accustomed to roam all over these hills quite alone, and I shall only be taking you out of your road."</p>
<p>"You cannot do that. I have no road to be taken out of ! My offer is not entirely disinterested. I am lost, like one of the babes in the wood. I have not the faintest notion as to where I am. Perhaps, if I go as far as Captain Noote's, he will give me a guide of some sort ? "</p>
<p>"Perhaps so," she assented rather dubiously ; "and I think I must be starting ; it gets dark so soon.</p>
<p>The chokidar tracked them down the terrace and between the wet hedges out into the grass-grown avenue. He had the craving of his kind for bucksheesh, and was prepared to follow the sahib for miles ; but the sahib happily divined his intentions, and threw him a couple of rupees, a donation that made his old visage wrinkle with glee.</p>
<p>"Ah," he muttered, as he saw them pass along, among
<pb n="127 AFTER THE STORM."/>
the tea bushes, " I knew that some sahib log would come and find her out, for she has a voice like running water, a face like the young moon. She will go and never return. She will cross the Kala Pani with him, and poor old Krookia will get no more rupees."</p>
<p>Meanwhile the young couple descended the hill side by side. The ground was wet, but the grasses, for which the place was famous, lifted their feathery heads as if merely refreshed by the storm, and thousands of others, resembling small snowballs, looked whiter than ever after the recent rain. 11rhat had been meek little runnells stealing timidly down the hillsides, were now bold, brawling torrents of dirty water, flecked with solid blocks of creamy foam, racing tumultuously towards the valley.</p>
<p>The avenue soon gave place to the cart track, and the track to a narrow path along the side of a ridge, ov erliuug with straggling brambles. Narrow as it was, Gerald contrived to walls abreast with his companion, whose pretty, slender feet dot over the ground with practised ease. The sun was setting gorgeously, as if summoning the world, and saying, " Behold the finest sunset that has ever been 1 " Here and there lie touched a range, and turned it into dazzling gold ; and here and there the beaks he had abandoned looked grey and deathlike&#x2014;like mountain ghosts. On the left hand, a fringe of pines--storm-worn veterans&#x2014;stood out in sharp black outline against a glowing orange sky. All around was silence&#x2014;the intense silence of the great lonely hills at nightfall, and there was not a sound to be heard, save the rushing of the streams and the murmur of two young voices.</p>
<p>" So you have been nearly a year in this part of the world, Miss Carwithen," said her escort. " Don't you find it desperately slow ? Yon have not even our resources. You do not smoke or shoot ! "</p>
<p>"No ; but I sew, and I read a good deal, and write letters, and try to keep up my French and German."</p>
<p>" And when your literary labours are over, what do you do ? "</p>
<p>" I go for long walks with my dog."</p>
<p>" And after your rambles ? "</p>
<pb n="128 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>

<p>" I go home to tea, as I am doing now, and I read aloud to Captain Noote, and I play backgammon."</p>
<p>"I suppose he is an old fogey ? " he asked suspiciously.</p>
<p>" Oh yes ; 'as old as the hills,' his wife says. I dare say he is seventy."</p>
<p>" A gentleman ? "</p>
<p>"What do you call a gentleman?" she rejoined, with a smile.</p>
<p>"Let me see," taking off his cap, and running his fingers through his crisp locks. "Position, breeding, education, and money are supposed to make one ; but my idea of a gentleman is a man who respects himself, and makes others do the same."</p>
<p>" Do you know that your definition would apply to hundreds of people around us ? "</p>
<p>"Yes. Quite true. I know many gentlemanly men earning four or five rupees a month. My own bearer has the air and manner of the old <hi rend="italic">regime</hi>; and Captain Noote ? "</p>
<p>" Has not the air and manner of the old <hi rend="italic">regime</hi>, but you Rill soon see him, and then judge of him for yourself."</p>
<p>"What a diplomatic reply. Has he no marked peculiarities ? "</p>
<p>"He wears a fur cap always. He never ceases asking questions&#x2014;idle, unnecessary questions ; and he eats his eggs raw&#x2014;a horrible spectacle !&#x2014;and he gobbles over his food."</p>
<p>"Well, I myself like an egg that has been merely carried throuuh the kitchen, and though I don't wear a fur cap, I am afraid I am always asking questions too, am I not ? "</p>
<p>"You have not asked me about Mrs. Noote," she answered evasively.</p>
<p>"No ; for I believe I have seen her with my own eyes at our sports. We met, 'twas in a crowd, and she wore no wreath of roses, but cape of cat-skins, and rode a big grey pony, which she was whacking with her umbrella."</p>
<p>" Yes, that was Mrs. Noote," said Juliet, with a smile.</p>
<p>" I am ashamed of being always in the interrogative mood, Miss Carwibhen ; but you can retaliate, and ask <hi rend="italic">me</hi> questions."</p>
<p>"Yes, thank you. But I really don't want to know
<pb n="129 AFTER THE STORM."/>
anything about you! Oh, I don't mean to be so rude. Please forgive me. I did not quite mean that ; but I shall never see you again, after to-day ; and&#x2014;and &#x2014;"</p>
<p>Pray don't be sure of that," he interrupted. " I fancy you and I will meet often." It was Kismet. He had discovered Lady Juliet for himself, and was firmly determined not to lose sight of her.</p>
<p>"Mrs. Noote does not care for strangers," said Juliet, gravely.</p>
<p>" Perhaps she may care for me. I am generally a great favourite with old ladies."</p>
<p>" But she does not consider herself old. Have you any sisters ? " continued the girl, wishing to make up for her late unfortunate remark.</p>
<p>" No, not one ; not even a sister-in-law."</p>
<p>" And are you not sorry ? "</p>
<p>" No ; I cannot say that I miss them. I generally get on very well with other fellows' sisters."</p>
<p>" Do you really ? " was her simple exclamation. " And where have you been since you came out from home?"</p>
<p>" First to a camp, and then I went on a survey trip to the frontier ; it was rather a long and tough business. The precipices were awful. We could only get forward like natives, in our bare feet, and at the rate of ten miles a day ; the route was scarcely practicable."</p>
<p>"What was the road like ? Anything like this&#x2014;a narrow, slippery path ? "</p>
<p>"There was none in many parts ; in others, round the face of sheer precipices, there were wooden supports introduced into the rock, and covered with slabs of stone, about two feet wide&#x2014;a drop of about three thousand feet. You crawled along like a fly on the side of a house. Ah, that was a bad bit, especially where, in some places, the wood bad rotted away, and a tree trunk was flung across, and you had to get over on it as best you could, with a ghastly lookin; drop, and a river roaring for you below ! "</p>
<p>" You must have a good head ? "</p>
<p>"Yes, luckily for me. I don't often lose it.&#x2014;But, by Jove, I've lost it now," he added to himself.
" And when you got to the frontier, what did you find ? "</p>

<pb n="130 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>

<p>"Oh, frontier men in Pig-tails and filthy sheepskin coats and long snow-shoes. They carried spears, and bows and arrows, and slings. They could sling stones for sixty yards. They made some grand shots at <hi rend="italic">me</hi>. They were almost savages, and live on the flesh of wild horses."</p>
<p>"And what did you live on ? "</p>
<p>" Very little indeed ; dal and attar, and now and then some yak beef. We couldn't manage much baggage, you see."</p>
<p>" Yes ; I am surprised you got back alive. Where did you go to next ? "</p>
<p>"To Tamashabad&#x2014;a gay station on the plains ; but it was not particularly gay when I was there. I spent most of my time attending funerals. By the way, talking of gaiety, I suppose you will not always live in these wilds, but soon emerge into what is called `the world' ? "</p>
<p>" I hope to go to England next year. I have some kind friends there who are going to send for me."</p>
<p>" But, Mr. Carwithen ?&#x2014;Pardon me, I am incorrigible ; you must find me worse than Captain Noote ! "</p>
<p>He had an intense desire to find out why this beautiful girl had been consigned to the wilds of Kumaon, and he could not keep his thoughts from finding speech.</p>
<p>" I suppose you rarely meet any one in your rambles ? "</p>
<p>" Yes ; I know a great number of hill people, especially about our own village. I can speak the dialect, and we are very good friends. I make their children bead-necklaces, and jackets out of scraps of my old dresses ; and they tell me stories, and give me presents of berries and mush-rooms."</p>
<p>"Berries and mushrooms ! You don't mean to say you <hi rend="italic">eat</hi> them ? "</p>
<p>"What ! wild strawberries and raspberries ? Certainly I do."</p>
<p>"And live to tell the tale."</p>
<p>" Yes, and to heat many tales. The lives of the people are just as exciting as elsewhere, though you might not suppose it. They have ambitions, and jealousies, and feuds, and love affairs. Last autumn, too, there was a horrible tragedy."</p>
<pb n="131 AFTER THE STORM."/>
<p>"What was it about? But I need not ask. Land or a woman."</p>
<p>"How could you guess ? It <hi rend="italic">was</hi> all about a beautiful girl. She cost two men their lives. Purooli lives in the village still ; but, handsome as she is, I hate to look at her."</p>
<p>"Don't you like looking at handsome people ? <hi rend="italic">I</hi> do," he remarked with emphasis.</p>
<p>"No, no. Not when their face has been the cause of several deaths ! "</p>
<p>"Ah, she is a sort of Hill Helen&#x2014;` many drew swords and fought. Where'er I came, I brought calamity.' There, you were nearly down ! " he exclaimed. " You had better hold on to me."</p>
<p>"No, thank you," she replied, with a rather derisive smile ; but next moment she was thankful to snatch at an outstretched grasp.</p>
<p>Sure-footed as she was, she found it difficult to proceed. The track was a mere goat-path at the best of times, and had become positively dangerous from slips in the recent storm.</p>
<p>Gulab Singh, a herdsman in quest of two strayed kids, paused as he encountered the Miss Sahib, and stared hard from beneath his vet, brown blanket as he saw her being helped down the hill by a youu, and active sahib&#x2014;a sahib from a pultoon in Durano, without doubt ! He squatted on his heels, and looked after the pair in amazement. He would have been still more surprised if he could have understood what the sahib was saying to himself, as he held the girl's slim fingers within his own. He was saying--</p>
<p>If I have any luck, I am now holding the hand of Mrs. Gerald Romiilly, my future wife;" and he involuntarily tightened his grasp upon it with that conviction.</p>
<p>It was almost dark ; they could no longer distinguish each other's faces. The sun was gone ; the stars were out ; the fireflies danced in the purple gloom it was time that Juliet was at home. Once in the valley, she set off at the top of her speed, for she feared, as well she might, the wrath of Mrs. Noote.</p>
<p>" Where can that girl be ? " Mrs. Noote had querulously demanded of her husband. " She went to Trafford's Rest four hours ago ? "</p>
<pb n="132 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
<p>" Well, she can't come to much harm <hi rend="italic">there</hi>--can she?" said the captain.</p>
<p>" No. I don't mind her spending her days there ! It's better than racing hot foot to Durano or the Cassons, and meeting young men, and getting her head turned. She's bad enough the day she gets her English letters ! "</p>
<p>"Well, I declare, I think she is wonderfully contented ; she never asks for aught, not even to go to church ; she has no diversion, no company of any kind, and she is here nigh a year. She's a good girl."</p>
<p>" She ain't much trouble, that I'll allow ; and she is a water-drinker, and has done up my bonnets beautiful, and is a great help with the butter ; but I always feel she is brooding over some plan in her head, that she is looking forward to some scheme."</p>
<p>"No, no ; nairy a bit. What scheme could she have ? It's <hi rend="italic">you</hi> that has the plan. I wonder how she will take to it ? " And he took his pipe off the chimney-piece, and left the room chuckling.</p>
<p>" Seven o'clock&#x2014;not a sign of that girl ! " repeated Mrs. Noote, for the third time. She was getting seriously uneasy regarding the safety of her precious sixty pounds a year. Had Juliet fallen down a khud, or been gored by a buffalo ? Ah ! there was the click of the gate at last, and the sound of voices. She hurried into the verandah, lamp in hand.</p></div>
<div id="018" type="chapter" n="18"><head>CHAPTER XVIII
<lb/>AN UNWELCOME QUEST.
<lb/>"I leave my character behind me."
<lb/><hi rend="italic">School for Scandal.</hi></head>
<p>"WELL, I truly never did I" cried Mrs. Noote, in her sharpest key. " Call <hi rend="italic">this</hi> an hour for a respectable girl to be coming home ! "</p>
" I could not come any sooner," rejoined Juliet, boldly advancing into the light ; and her hostess was startled to see that she was accompanied by a good-looking young
<pb n="133 AN UNWELCOME GUEST."/>
man&#x2014;evidently an officer and gentleman. This was beyond everything ; where had she picked him up ?</div>
<p>"Mrs. Noote, this is Mr. Romilly," said the girl, introducing him as coolly as if she brought home a companion every night of her life.</p>
<p>" I met him at the `Rest.' Perhaps you can spare a coolie to show him the road to Durano ; he has lost his way."
<p>" Oh, of course&#x2014;of course I" replied Mrs. Noote, only too delighted to be rid of him so quickly ; and, raising- her voice, she called, " Rutton Singh."</p>
<p>"What's up now ? What makes Miss Judy so late ? " inquired Captain Noote, as he shuffled out, pipe in mouth. " Oh I "&#x2014;catching sight of the stranger, he snatched away his pipe and touched his cap&#x2014;" your servant, sir. What can we do for you ? "</p>
<p>"I've been out shooting, and have missed my way. If you can give me a guide to Durano, I shall be obliged."</p>
<p>Surely, sir, surely, it's seven long miles, but we will manage it ; but come in--come in ; we are just sitting down to a bite of supper. Cold pig's check and soda cakes, if you'll condescend to our humble fare."</p>
<p>Condescend to sit at table with Lady Juliet I Needless to say, he accepted the invitation with effusion.</p>
<p>Mrs. Noote said nothing. She was excessively put out ; once Noote came across a gentleman, lie was merely an old sergeant saluting and " sir-ing," and letting himself down. However, she would give it to him to-morrow, encouraging officers about the place, when he knew her plans for William John, and him coming up in three weeks. Just for the present she dissembled her feelings, and went in search of another cup and saucer, and a pair of candles ; but she subsequently dispensed hospitality with an unpleasant straight line between her brows, and her lips so tightly closed that they might have been pasted together.</p>
<p>Meanwhile Juliet, who had retired to change her wet skirts and shoes, now joined the company in a soft white dress, with her hair freshly arranged. She was surprised to find Mr. Romilly (artful wretch) in brisk conversation with his hostess ; his cheeriness had proved irresistible ; he was young, merry, and handsome ; and, after all, Airs. Noote
<pb n="134 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
was a woman&#x2014;but a shrewd one, too. Not once did she suffer him to make a remark to Juliet, who was looking quite unnecessarily pretty. She herself answered, or fore-stalled every one of his questions, and poured forth a flow of ceaseless chatter. She miglit bridle his tongue, and prevent his speaking to bliss Carwithen, but she could not control his eyes ; and, whilst Mrs. Noote gabbled away about I)nrano society, he was stealing many glances at her lodger. Yes ; the slender hands, delicate nostrils, fine lines of chin and brow, were all unmistakable signs of race. He was more than ever amazed to see this brilliant creature anii(I such surroundings ; he dawdled purposely, so as to prolong the meal ; but at last it was over, and Mrs. Noote, as she left the room, displayed considerable tactical ability by calling to Juliet to follow her. "'There was a note for her to write, and she had better leave the gentlemen to have their smoke."</p>
<p>Captain Noote now hastened to place a square bottle containing whisky on the table ; also a goblet of water, two tumblers, and his pipe, and assured his guest that he was not to think of stirring&#x2014;lie must wait for the moon.</p>
<p>Young Romilly was anxiously awaiting the return of Juliet, so he gladly lit a cheroot, poured out some mild whisky and water, and let the old man " bukh," whilst his glance wandered round the apartment. What a den I What a home for this refined and well-bred girl ! What a couple of companions ! He noted the rickety furniture, the stained walls, and the lack of the ordinary comforts of life. What had Miss Carwithen done to deserve such a retreat ? What a contrast to her father's luxurious home !</p>
<p>The old sergeant at his elbow, with his red shiny face, and coarse, weather-beaten features, was imbibing strong potations, and becoming each instant more garrulous and more communicative.</p>
<p>You see, it's unusual for us to take in boarders," he explained, apropos of nothing ; " but the missus, she said she wanted a companion, and we had a spare room ; and sixty pounds a year ain't no blind nut ! She's no trouble or expense ; just takes what's going, and has no extras or luxuries."
<pb n="135 AN UNWELCOME GUEST."/>

<p>"No ; she certainly has not," acquiesced his listener, emphaticially.
<p>It suits all parties," continued the old man, on whom the sarcasm was completely lost. "It suits her folks, and it suits us."</p>
<p>How did you hear of the young lady? for I don't suppose she heard of <hi rend="italic">you</hi>."
<p>"There you've said it ! That's what she is&#x2014;a young lady, and not a bit proud, though 'Liza differs. She goes among the Pahari folk, and talks and plays with the children. I've seen her herding goats ere now, and picking- wood for old women. She makes out all Eliza's butter-bills beautifully, and goes her messages. She dusts this room, and saddles her own pony. No ; she is  <hi rend="italic">not</hi> proud ; but I won't deny she is a dainty little hussy ! When the mountain streams are coming down yellow and muddy, would you believe that she strains every drop of her bath-water herself through a calico bag ? She won't eat coarse bits, or fat, or onions. No ; she'd rather have chupatties, and she would not touch whisky to save her life ! She's mortal particular about her handkerchiefs, and her shoes and stockings. Lord ! what a foot it is ! and what lovely ankles ! and--"</p>
<p>"But how did you come across her?" interrupted his listener, impatiently.</p>
<p>"Well, you see, Mr. Carwithen married a sergeant's daughter."</p>
<p>" Mr. Carwithen&#x2014;married&#x2014;a&#x2014;sergeant's daughter," repeated young Romilly very slowly, as if he could not believe his ears.</p>
<p>" Ay, a mighty pretty girl, but no more use nor a wax doll, nor half as amusing. She died, and left this child a baby, and Carwithen smuggled her off to a boarding-school, and clipped half her name, and went home, and never came back, and won't take her with him at no price. He is ashamed of her&#x2014;that's the size of it ; though <hi rend="italic">I'd</hi> be proud to have such a daughter."</p>
<p>"I should rather think you would," said the young man sarcastically ; " but you have not told me how you cawn across her."
<pb n="136 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
<p>"Well, my step-son knows a clerk in Skippers', the agents, and he heard as how they were looking for a home for a young woman, very quiet and retired (with a wink), and so he mentioned us, and she came a year ago ; and here she is, hard and fast."</p>
<p>" So it would seem ; but I suppose she will leave you some day ! "</p>
<p>"Maybe not. She'll have two thousand pounds down "&#x2014;putting his hand to his mouth, and speaking in a hoarse, mysterious whisper&#x2014;" and Eliza&#x2014;that's my wife&#x2014;thinks as she would be the very ticket for William John ! "</p>
<p>The old man had taken too much whisky, or he never would have divulged such a family secret ; but with a lingering gleam of caution, he added, " Don't go to ever let on as I told you ! William John is a clerk, a sub-assistant on the E.I.R.&#x2014;a promising chap, with shoulders like a buffalo, and a hand the size of a ham. Eliza calls Miss Judy her niece ; and she will be her daughter-in-law, as sure as you sit there ! But mind you never let on."</p>
<p>" You may make your mind perfectly easy on that score," said his listener, with prompt sternness. " You must be joking. It is impossible that a young lady--"</p>
<p>He paused. Why argue with this wretched whisky-sodden old creature ?</p>
<p>"Impossible that a grand young madam would marry Billy John ! " with a maudlin chuckle. " Wait till you see him&#x2014;a strapping lad, with a moustache, and evening clothes as good as your own, and a hundred and twenty rupees a month. After all, she is only old Smithson's granddaughter, and her folks main anxious to get rid of her. It would be a good thing to have her married to a respectable man ! "</p>
<p>"And is Miss Carwithen really Mrs. Noote's niece?"</p>
<p>" I'll explain <hi rend="italic">that</hi>," confidentially ;&#x2014;taking an immense gulp of whisky and water. "When we heard as she was connected with old Smithson&#x2014;we knew him long ago&#x2014;Eliza sits down and makes a draft of a letter, saying as <hi rend="italic">we</hi> were Smithson's  relations too. She was main keen for the money, and she thought this would clinch the business. She made
<pb n="137 AN UNWELCOME GUEST."/>
me copy out all she wrote, and we sent it to Skipper's, and they just hopped at her offer ; d'ye see ? "</p>
<p>" Yes," nodded Gerald, apparently deeply interested, as be leant his head on his hand, and steadily surveyed the old soldier with a pair of handsome dark-grey eyes.</p>
<p>Of course we are all related in Adam ; that's how Eliza got out of it ; but still, I don't say as she ought to give out as Miss Judy's her niece, when she is no more her niece than she is yours. And they are not a bit alike ; indeed, any one might see as she was a bit above <hi rend="italic">us</hi>. So what's the good of making believe, and telling lies ? "</p>
<p>" What, indeed ? " repeated his listener contemptuously.</p>
<p>"Of course it seems mortal queer, a young lady living here in this sort of wilderness, and never a friend to come nigh her, and no belongings whatever. At first I suspected it might be madness, or fits ; but there's no sign of either. I cannot say how she carried on at the school ; and she looks just what she is&#x2014;an innocent child ; but looks is deceitful. Still waters run deep,' and maybe	--?</p>
<p>"Stop! " shouted the young man, bringing down his fist on the table, with a bang that made the whisky-bottle stagger. "How dare you speculate in this way about an inoffensive, friendless young lady, you drunken old rascal ? "</p>
<p>" Heyday ! Well, I truly never did ! What's all this ? " inquired Mrs. Noote, in her shrillest key, as she entered. "Pretty doings, calling a man names in his own house."</p>
<p>"That is true," admitted Gerald, " and I beg his pardon ; he is all old man. I have a hot temper, and forgot myself ; but he said something that riled me, and I dropped on him."</p>
<p>" Ay, and so he has been <hi rend="italic">talking</hi>, has he? "&#x2014; sternly surveying her drowsy husband. " Just like the old Poggle," and she glanced at the whisky&#x2014;now at a very low ebb. " He has been making an ass of himself as usual. Noote, go to bed vshe added, raising her voice authoritatively. "Off now, without a word ! As for <hi rend="italic">you</hi>, sir," to Gerald, " the moon is up, the coolie is waiting to show you your road home, and I hope you may never find your way back to these parts. We want no gentry about the place, calling honest folk names ! Don't let me ever see your face here
<pb n="138 A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
again." And with this polite valediction, candle in hand, she waved him out.</p>
<p>Yes, there was his guide, wrapped in a brown blanket, carrying; his gun and. game-bag. But where was Juliet ? It was evident that he had not the smallest chance of bidding her adieu ; but he bade Mrs. Noote a fair good night, with exa&#x2014;Crated civility, and took his departure. The extreme politeness of his leave-taking infuriated his hostess ten times more than if he had deliberately insulted her, and she shook her fist furiously after his retreating figure, and muttered a bad word.</p>
<p>As he tramped seven long miles back to his quarters he was never aware of the roughness of the road, the steepness of the hills, much less the distance. IIe seemed to get over the ground by magic, for his thoughts were full of Juliet Carwithen ; he was resolved to emancipate her from the Nootes, root and branch&#x2014;but how ? This was the problem that he was working out in his brain, as he covered the ground with a speed that left the nimble-footed hill-man panting after him in vain.</p>
<p>Old Noote and his confidences and his whisky, shrewd bars. Noote, with her fierce eye and shrill tongue, were sufficiently detestable, but the member of the family that he hated most bitterly was William John&#x2014;the wonld-be suitor for this dainty, elegant, refined girl&#x2014;a ticket-collector on the E.I.R., a monster with a hand like a ham ! Juliet was not likely to yield to such a fate without a struggle ; at any rate, it was his mission to rescue her&#x2014;his&#x2014;and oh, happy thought ! oh, <hi rend="italic">second</hi> thought&#x2014;his and Sophy Carson's. he could not move in the enterprise alone ; Sophy would be home in a week's time, and there was not a day to be lost.</p>

not ask him here, she answered. "At any rate, I am not likely to see him again."</p>

<p>SOPHY CASSON generally spent all the winter in the plains. She had now been from home for eight months paying a round of visits ; she had even been to Australia. Among various new scenes, she had almost completely forgotten bars. Noote and Dirs. Noote's niece. Within thirty-six hours of her return to Snow View, she received a visit from Gerald Romilly. <div id="020" type="chapter" n="20"><head>CHAPTER, XX.
<lb/>TO READ FRENCH. <lb/>"Ici on parle francais."</head>
<p>IT is a great advantage to have an ally who is prompt in action, and a woman of her word. The very day after her cousin's visit saw Mrs. Casson and her bay pony standing at Dirs. Noote's wicket gate. Juliet was at home on this occasion, and delighted to see the visitor. She threw down an old coat she was mending, and flew to open the gate, helped the rider to dismount, and conducted her indoors. Mrs. Noote accepted the call entirely as a compliment to herself ; she talked of the season's crops, her salt butter, and the prevalence of small-pox among her poultry, scarcely permitting Juliet and Mrs. Casson to exchange a word. At last she rose to get out tea, as she never trusted her keys to any one, and the two ladies were left alone for a short time.</p>
<p>" I have come over specially to see you, Miss Carwithen," began Mrs. Casson.</p>
<p>It is very good of you ; but how did you know that my name was Carwithen ? " inquired Juliet, with rising colour.</p>
<p>"My cousin, Mr. Romilly, told me. It seems that lie knows your people at home. Have you seen much of him?"</p>
<p>" I have met him once," she answered rather coldly.</p>
<p>"Only <hi rend="italic">once</hi>!" Mrs. Casson mentally ejaculated ; "I thought Gerald knew her very well."</p>
<p>"What a dull time you must have had here all this year ! I suppose you have never been away ? " she said aloud.</p>
<p>No ; except twice down to Durano for Sundays."</p>
<p>"And do you know any one there ? Do people come up to see you ? "</p>
<pb n="149 TO READ FRENCH."/>

<p>"No ; I know no one except Mr. and Mrs. Rozario."</p>
<p>Mr. and Mrs. Rozario !" ejaculated the visitor in a high key of astonishment. " Well, I think it is time you made some other acquaintances, and had a little change. Would you care to come and stay with me ? "</p>
<p>" Thank you, I should, very much indeed ; but Mrs. Noote won't--"</p>
<p>Mrs. Noote <hi rend="italic">will</hi>," interrupted the other. "Just leave her to me. I shall begin by asking her to bring you to spend a day ; then I shall borrow you <hi rend="italic">alone</hi>; then I shall induce her to spare you to me for a long visit."</p>
<p>Enter Mrs. Noote with a plate of stale biscuits and a pair of suspicious eyes. <hi rend="italic">What</hi> had these two been talking about ? What nonsense had Dirs. Casson been pouring into the girl's ears ?&#x2014;her eyes were dancing in her head ! She was extremely relieved and surprised when her visitor said, in her most persuasive manner--</p>
<p>"Mrs. Noote, I want you to come and spend a long day with me this week ; and, of course, to bring Miss Carwithen."
<p>" Carwithen ! " echoed Mrs. Noote, rather shrilly.</p>
<p>" Yes ; don't you think her own name is prettier than Carr ? "</p>
<p>" Her father does not think so," rejoined the other tartly.</p>
<p>"That may be ; but every one should be called by their real name, otherwise it leads to misunderstandings. And now about this day, Dirs. Noote. I want you to come down and see my new IIansee cow, and tell me what you think of her ; and I will give you a sitting of black Spanish eggs."</p>
<p>Here was indeed a bribe ! Black Spanish eggs were valuable, and it was known that Mrs. Casson was exceedingly chary of hers.</p>
<p>" Well, thank you ; I'd like to come. A day's outing will do me good. I can't come on Thursday, it's butter day ; but I'll see about Saturday, if that will suit ; and I'm to bring Juliet ? "</p>
<p>" Oh yes, please," with assumed indifference.</p>
<p>" Well, in that case, I must borrow the Bunnia's pony, and we will be with you about twelve o'clock." And so the matter was settled.</p>
<p>On Saturday Mrs. Noote was to be seen walking mincingly
<pb n="150	A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
about Mrs. Casson's garden, holding a green-silk parasol over her sandy head, and talking in a whispering voice, and firmly persuaded that she was conducting herself in the most lady-like manner, and that Mrs. Casson had suddenly taken a fancy to her, and would be the means of introducing her to the best society in Durano. Already she saw herself lunching at the Parsonage, and patronizing the Rozarios. She would get Juliet to turn her black silk at once, and send to Chotah Bilat for a veil and new gloves. As for Juliet, she seemed absorbed in papers and magazines, and quite neglected, whilst Mrs. Casson made much of <hi rend="italic">her</hi>, and escorted her round the cowsheds and poultry-yards. As Mrs. Casson placed thirteen beautiful white eggs in a basket, she said in a careless way--</p>
<p>"Your young friend must find it a little dull ?"</p>
<p>" Oh, lord, no ! not half as dull as school," replied Mrs. Noote, emphatically.</p>
<p>" I suppose she is pretty well educated?"</p>
<p>"<hi rend="italic">Pretty</hi> well ! Lawks ! why, she talks French and German just the same as you and I do English ; ay, and reads and writes them, too, and thinks in them."</p>
<p>"Indeed ! How wonderfully clever she must be ! Then, Mrs. Noote, I want you to do me a small favour," handing her the basket of eggs as she spoke.</p>
<p>"Why, certainly, anything," rejoined Mrs. Noote, in the fulness of her heart. If she succeeded in raising black Spanish fowl, she would get twenty rupees apiece for them.</p>
<p>" You know I am rather lonely out here," continued Mrs. Casson.</p>
<p>Well, I'm sure you're welcome to come up and see <hi rend="italic">me</hi> any day, and I'll drop in on you whenever I can spare an hour ; only say the word," rejoined Mrs. Noote with effusion.</p>
<p>" Oh, thanks ; but what I mean is, that I have a good deal of idle time I'd like to employ profitably ; my French is horribly rusty, and I'd be so much obliged to you if you would allow Miss Carwithen to come over and read and speak it with me, say once a week ? "</p>
<p>Mrs. Noote breathed hard, and regarded her hostess withh a look of searching interrogation.</p>
<pb n="151 TO READ FRENCH."/>
<p>Mrs. Casson's sudden desire for improving herself struck her as extraordinary.</p>
<p>" She teach <hi rend="italic">you</hi> ! " she grunted incredulously.</p>
<p>Yes," with a sample, unabashed air ; " since you your-self tell me that she is such a wonderful proficient." Alas ! there was poor Mrs. Noote entangled and driven into a corner by her own boastful words. "It would be an immense advantage to me," pleaded this cunning woman ; " that is, if you can spare her."</p>
<p>" Oh," with ail air of lofty condescension, "I dare say I can <hi rend="italic">spare</hi> her. But how is she to get here ? It's nigh on five miles ! "</p>
<p>" I will send my pony half-way."</p>
<p>" I see you are heist on it," said Mrs. Noote, crossly ; and there was a sort of gleam in her eye as she said, "Mind you don't go turning her head ; and she's not to get to know any young men, not at no price."</p>
<p>After all, this going and coming would cement the intimacy between the houses of Noote and Casson, and Mrs. Noote liked to feel that she was conferring a favour, especially a favour that cost her nothing ; not even the loan of her pony. So it was all arranged, much to Juliet's delight and astonishment ; she could scarcely believe her ears when Mrs. Casson took her aside, and whispered confidentially to her that she was to come over and spend a day at Snow View once a week, and that she expected her next Saturday without fail.</p>
<p>The two ponies and their riders had scarcely turned the elbow of the hill, when Gerald Romilly came galloping up from the opposite direction.
" Just in time to be late ! " cried his cousin, vivaciously. "'Mrs. Noote and Miss Carwithen are scarcely out of sight ; they have been spending a long and happy day with <hi rend="italic">me</hi>."</p>
<p>" Nonsense ! " jumping off his pony	and how did they spend it?"</p>
<p>"Mrs. Noote poked about the place, and asked questions, and lectured me on my extravagance, and engrossed me altogether, and dropped her h's, and washed her face in her finger-glass, and was disgustingly familiar and intimate, and altogether enjoyed herself very much indeed."</p>
<pb n="152	A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
<p>"And Juliet "&#x2014;he coloured, and added&#x2014;" oh, I mean Miss Carwithen&#x2014;did she enjoy herself ?"</p>
<p>" Yes, in skimming through the new magazines, and playing the piano. She is quite easily amused ; I could not talk to her much, as Mrs. Noote was so monopolizing and suspicious. However, she is coming here by herself frequently."</p>
<p>"You are not in earnest ? I thought Mrs. Noote would not trust her out of her sight. Why is she allowing her to come?"</p>
<p>"She is coming once a week, as a great favour, in order to complete my education, and to <hi rend="italic">teach me French</hi>," rejoined Sophy, demurely.</p>
<p>"Sophy ! " cried her cousin, with a shout of laughter, "you are the cleverest woman in the world."</p>
<p>" Still," with twinkling eyes, " my French and German are very rusty."</p>
<p>" Did the old woman make any stipulation ? Was there no bargain ? " he asked, as he took a turn up and down the verandah.</p>
<p>"No, not what you'd call a bargain ; I gave a sitting of Spanish eggs and some flower-seeds, and I have promised and vowed not to turn the girl's head ; and you, Jerry, must promise me the same."</p>
<p>"Not to turn Miss Carwithen's head ! She is a good deal more likely to turn mine ! What day does she come to you ? "</p>
<p>"I shall not tell you ; you want to know too much."</p><p> "'Then I shall come and spend a week here," he answered with decision, " and find out ! "</p>
<p>" Don't be silly, Gerald. I have secured Miss Carwithen's company for myself ; and you know very well that you are not a ladies' man."</p>
<p>" It's never too late to mend ! "</p>
<p>"Don't talk nonsense, but go away and get ready for dinner."</p>
<milestone unit="stars"/>
<p>There are a number of tea estates scattered over the humaon hills, and among planters Robbic Casson was considered a man of credit and renown ; and not merely in his
<pb n="153 TO READ FRENCH."/>
own neighbourhood. Casson's tea-chests, with their brand
mountain with three pears), commanded a fair price in Mincing Lane ; for Robbie understood his business, the pruning of gardens and nurseries, the manufacture of green and black tea, and the proper complexion and manipulation of the leaf&#x2014;although he had started in life with a totally different destination&#x2014;like not a few other young men. He had failed at Sandhurst, and was subsequently at his wit's end for a proi Iession, nay, a livelihood ; New Zealand, 'Kinnesota, South Africa, were proposed ; sheep, corn, gold, and diamonds were discussed in turn, and then Colonel Romilly, an old friend of his father's, suggested <hi rend="italic">tea</hi>&#x2014;a small estate in a good climate, healthy outdoor employment, absence of undesirable society, and a temptation to spend money. This advice was considered sound, and at twenty-two Robbie Casson set out to seek his fortune on the spurs of the Himalayas.</p>
<p>He liked the life, for he was his own master. He worked hard, and at the end of four years took a run home, a bronzed, good-looking, prosperous young fellow (for all his connections drank Casson's Kumaon tea). When at home, he fell in love with, and married, Sophy Clare, a pretty girl with a respectable fortune, and carried her away to his tea-garden, greatly to the dismay of her relations. "A girl with five hundred a year, who might have made an excellent match, to marry a penniless planter, and throw herself away in the wilds of the North West ! " But the bride elect turned a deaf ear to every remonstrance, and married Robbie, and departed loaded with wedding-presents, varying from a photograph-frame to a piano. Thus it came to pass, that Snow View was a somewhat glorified edition of a planter's bungalow : there was a garden and tennis-court ; the mistress of the house had her daily post, her staff of excellent servants, her pony, and her own way. There was no occasion to live in the rough, she said ; her <hi rend="italic">menage</hi> was dainty, her dresses simple, but by no means behind the fashion ; she went down to Durano frequently, she visited the plains, and now and then took a trip home. She was a person of influence and importance in her own circle, and Juliet Carwithen little guessed what a lucky girl she
<pb n="154	A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
might consider herself at being " taken up by Mrs. Casson." Some people thought Dir. Casson a heavy young man, with his big frame, slow speech, and lazy brown eyes. They said " that Dirs. Casson had the brains of the firm and certainly Robbie was not an eloquent or fluent talker, his tastes were sporting, not intellectual, and he rarely opened a book. Nevertheless, the Cassons agreed admirably. Dirs. Casson always quoted her husband's opinions with evident good faith, and he, on his part, believed that Sophy, like the king, " could do no wrong." She had a good deal of leisure time on her hands, and he was pleased to see that Sophy had recently discovered a charming young companion, a very pretty bright girl, who sang and talked, and walked and read with his wife whilst he was down at the tea-houses, or over the hills shooting birds, and stalking makor. At first she came once a week, now oftener, and he knew that it was the desire of his wife's heart to secure her for a lengthened visit.</p>
<p>With this end in view, mrs. Casson boldly rode up to Mrs. Noote's one afternoon, having previously sent peace-offerings of eggs, chickens, and young cabbage plants, to smooth the way.</p>
<p>After some conversation about the weather, the season, visitors in Durano, and Captain Noote's lumbago, firs. Casson said quite briskly, " Dirs. Noote, I have come to ask you to allow Juliet to spend a month with me."</p>
<p>Mrs. Noote drew down her upper lip, and shook her head solemnly.</p>
<p>"Runaway, Juliet, my child," continued her friend, "and put on your hat ; you must walk part of the way back with me. Meanwhile, I am going to talk Dirs. Noote over," and she nodded reassuringly.</p>
<p>" Now, Mrs. Noote," she began, in her pleasantest tone, " you keep the girl too close. She never leaves this except to spend a dull day with me. She never sees any one of her own age, or mixes with other young people."</p>
<p>" It's by her father's wish she is kept retired, and sees no company," rejoined Mrs. Noote, severely.</p>
<p>" Have you that down in black and white ? "</p>
<p>"No; it's understood, and I'm responsible."</p>
<pb n="155 TO READ FRENCH."/>
<p>"Yes ; but surely you won't object to her coming and paying me a little visit, and I will be responsible."</p>
<p>"No, no ; you'll be taking her to parties, and maybe having beaux about the house."</p>
<p>"You mean young men ?"</p>
<p>"Yes, such as that chap Romilly. How soon <hi rend="italic">he</hi> tracked out a pretty girl ! I forbid him this house ; but Lal Sing, the syce, says he is often out your way ; and last Saturday he walked back with Juliet here&#x2014;every step of the way ! "</p>
<p>" My husband was with him. Did Lal Sing mention that ? You don't mean to say that Juliet is never to speak to a man, or to mix in any society ? I think you make a mistake in keeping such a very tight hand over Juliet. She is past nineteen, and her own mistress. She could leave you, if she chose. Of course her allowance would cease ; but she has resources in herself and friends. She has always a firm
friend in <hi rend="italic">me</hi>."
<p>"And another in young Romilly," sneered Mrs. Noote. "However, she is under my thumb till she is one and twenty."</p>
<p>"Ah ! that foolish idea comes from living out of the world,' rejoined Mrs. Casson. " She is as free to go as I am ! "</p>
<p>" Well, if she quits my house, she loses her allowance," snapped Mrs. Noote.</p>
<p>" And so do you ! " retorted the other, triumphantly. Mrs. Noote had not looked at the matter in this light until now, and her face fell.</p>
<p>" I really think you had better give Juliet more liberty ! " continued Mrs. Casson, with the air of a friendly adviser, " or she will take it. I see she has no piano, no private sitting-room."</p>
<p>" Piano ! " broke in the other. " Private sitting-room ! Bless us and save us&#x2014;for sixty pounds a year ! Well, I
truly never <hi rend="italic">did</hi> ! "
<p>" But this is a cheap place. Seventy rupees a month goes a long way. And, at any rate, it will cost you nothing to allow her to spend a month with me. I ask no more at present."</p>
<p>" Well, as you seem so determined, and I will allow she likes you, I suppose she will have to go ; but, mind, for not
<pb n="156	A FAMILY LIKENESS."/>
a day longer than the month, for I've a particular reason for wanting her at home."</p>
<p>" Yes, I promise, and I'll take the greatest care of her ; and I will send my dandy for her the day after to-morrow."</p>
<p>" Very well ; but she can't be going; out with you this evening, then. I've a heap for her to do (butter accounts). And you will promise me to keep all those rascally young officers at arm's length ? "</p>
<p>Mrs. Casson laughed, and made no reply.</p>
" Well, if you don't promise "&#x2014;and Mrs. Noote's face became crimson, whilst her light eyebrows stood out in startling relief&#x2014;" not one step shall she go ! So take your choice."</p>
<p>" Very well, then, I promise. I'll give no parties ; but I mean to take her down to Durano for a day or two, just to see the place."</p>
<p>" Well&#x2014;well, I suppose you must do that, if you like ; but don't be hawking her about to dances." With this final clause the treaty was concluded without further hostilities.</p>
<p>"Mrs. Casson thought that perhaps it was all for the best that Juliet's escort had been denied to her ; for, within a mile of Kala Dara, she was rather startled to find Gerald Romilly sitting on a boulder, with a brace of chikor beside him, and a gun between his knees.</p>
<p>" What a felicitous coincidence ! " he cried, as she came in sight. " Well, what luck ? "</p>

<p>" <hi rend="italic">French</hi> leave &#x2014;eh ? " and he laughed. "What is Miss Juliet going to teach you now ? "</p>
<p>" She might teach you to treat me with proper respect, Jerry, only that you will see nothing of her."</p>
<p>" What do you mean?"	he inquired, suddenly becoming serious.</p>
<p>" I have made a solemn vow and promise to Mrs. Noote to keep all rascally officers at arm's length ; and are you not a rascally officer ? "</p>
<p>"Certainly not. I am your cousin ; I am one of the family. And why are soldiers to be tabooed ? "</p>

that individual.</p>
